RATING: 9
I highly enjoy the multiplayer variant, and moderately enjoy playing Nations solo. The game is complex enough -- and luck-less enough -- to reward skill and thought. There are many ways to win, and tactics can change quickly over the course of a game. Both long-term thinking and flexible, tactical reactions are rewarded. Plus, whether you're winning or losing at any given time, it's important to stay on your toes, because it's not game over until it's officially over. Comebacks are possible. Additionally, the nuanced A/B sides of the player nations and the varying cards based on skill added a second degree of depth worthy of applause. I found solo play to be a bit easy, and playing for the sake of points isn't as fun as playing for survival/dominance. The "shadow opponent" doesn't think, so it's probably a bit too easy to outmaneuver. However, it's still fun overall.

RATING: 8
6 plays / 3,4,5 players
LIKE: The player's tableau is where the meat of strategy is, allocating properly and keeping spots flexible. Many paths to win. Interesting ways to adjust the game for playing abilities and length. War and bad events give you enough time to react. Competing for the cards added good tension. plays smooth.

DISLIKE: long game (4p=3.5 to 4 hr). The randomness of cards can be streaky and deny a card you need. Can be a punishing game, frustrating players.

OVERALL: A nation management game, reacting to events. Subtle strategies, but a lot of thinking and hard decisions. Perfect amount of player interaction. The randomness of cards is not terrible, but it can mess up your goals. i wish the game was shorter, but this could be adjusted. The game gets better the more it is played.

RATING: 8.5
It's obvious why this is a darling: a zillion cards of which you'll typically see only a fraction in any given play. The simplicity of the actions belies the complexity of interactions between players. There is the limited pool of cards for purchase, but also the conflict in military values, stability and 'books' is very good between players. It does take some time to complete, but there is an anxious fun in seeing the next set of available progress cards. And oh the tension in keeping your civilization viable by balancing all resources needed! Everything runs lean - delightful!

EDIT: After several more plays, I'm having trouble finding any fault with this game. I love it! I'm also finding the 'B' side of civs to be curiously *easier* to play than the 'A' side.

EDIT: More plays in. The game is wearing a bit now. I still find it really good, but I've explored most of what it has to offer. Scoring high is a matter of tactics - taking advantage of opportunities presented - and little more than that. It remains tense to be sure!

RATING: 8
Fantastic civ-building game.  It's got a very Euro-y feel, but captures the theme pretty well.  It's a relatively straight-forward game with a fair helping of luck, but seems deep enough and strategic enough to be worth the long play time.  Having only played twice and not having used any of the "advanced" cards, I do feel like most of the cards are just slight variations on each other, but my hope is that the advanced cards will spice things up.  Plus, giving each card widely differing powers might send AP through the roof.  In my plays so far, AP has not been much of a problem and turns tend to go pretty quickly for the most part.

This is a game I feel has a lot of promise and I look forward to playing it more.

RATING: 7
Through The Ages redux.  The similarities are too plentiful to be ignored.  In fact, the overall gameplay and experience feels very much the same.  Granted, there are many differences (especially military, wars & TtA's culture track), but I would rather play TtA.

Nations is a solid civ game.  The cards drive the game.  Limited manpower.  A nice twist with different starts via the B-sides of the player mats.  And you don't see all of the cards in any age, not even close, meaning different games can and will have wildly different skews in card distribution.  Also, the events push-pull on EVERYTHING, which can be good, bad or ridiculously painful.  VP gain is *VERY* incremental.

I want to say Nations is a nice, faster, lighter take on Through The Ages.  Yes, I *want* to say that but I can't.  Because it isn't.  It doesn't play much faster.  It's not that much lighter.  And the warfare in Nations is actually *MORE* brutal than with TtA.

I'll play Nations at times, I suppose, but I'll probably be wondering why I'm not playing Through The Ages instead.

RATING: 10
Excellent game.  First game with 5 players lasted 3.5 hours.  The players were not hardcore gamers.  Everyone had a good time even though it was a long game. Player ages were 45, 35, 17, 16, 14.  1 male, 4 female.

Pros:
Easiest rulebook to read and find information ever.
Logical layout of bits.
Does not feel fiddly.
Single player was also enjoyable for me.  It is a "can you top your score" type of single player game.
Very quick set up of game and very quick take down.
Leaves you with the "I want to play again" feeling after playing it.
Tactical players will enjoy.

Cons:
Game bits could be better quality, but are not as bad as some people say.
You may not be able to "play it again" after playing it because of the length of time to play.
Strategic players will not enjoy.

I have not played through the ages, but I have played 7 wonders.  This game feels like 7 Wonders in spirit.  This is the first worker placement game I've played/gotten.

update:  playing this 2 player is pretty enjoyable.  I play this with one of my nieces often.  She likes it. It takes about 1 hr as a 2 player game, pretty consistently.

RATING: 8
An excellent worker placement game. Lots of thematic flavor. 

The multitude of cards meant that, no 2 games will play the same. Though it may seem that there are lots of options in each and every turn, but it's not. It's up to the player to decide how he/she would like to bring his nation towards which victory pathway. From that decision, to formulate your play/plan towards that goal.

Significant player interaction in War especially, so players  can't concentrate on their own options only and forgo some sacrifices..

Overall, a cut above Lords Waterdeep and such similar worker placement type game. Highly replayable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Nations is a neat civ themed game. Although, mechanically, I don't think the gameplay really drives home the civ theme. The central board tracks stability, military strength, wars, and various random events. So that's all very civilization-y. But Nations is primarily a tableau building euro game with emphasis on worker allocation and gathering/managing resources. The main and plain of the gameplay is purchasing cards that come out onto the public market which gets filled up twice per age.

Unfortunately, the pacing of the game can be very strange since the things that come out on the market are randomly drawn and you might not see very many cards for the things that you *should* be seeing a lot of, statistically, and vice versa. The events are also pretty random and can be brutal. On the other hand, this randomness enhances replayability and the narrative of each game session. 

I also wish that there were more starting civs as well. The included nations are cool, but extremely limited (there are so few). Regardless, it's a very good game.

RATING: 9.25
I will start by saying that Nations is not a civ building game but a euro-game. Despite the wars, the game is a fairly complex worker placement rewarding the player with the most flexible strategy, the one who can make the best use of the cards appearing each round. The military could be very well be called "resource" and it would not change the game at all, but this just proves that it's euro. It had the potential to be a 9.5 or even a 10, but it has two drawbacks - the wars which are simply punishing without affecting the strongest player (no catching up mechanism there) and some of the cards are way too powerful. 

Nations remains one of the best games from Essen 2013 and it's high ratings and buzz are well deserved.

EDIT: Rating changed to 9.25 after 4 more games. Why? The game is faster than written on the box when you play with experienced players, I am rating it as a euro rather than a civ, the high replay value, no major imbalance.

RATING: 9
The advantage of the first player is extremaly unballanced much more than in e.g. Caylus. Drawed Egyptians and was the last player in order of the first round. The first player built some soldiers than took colonies cheaply boosting his production than played a war so at the end of the first round we could say that the play is finished. Really frustrating :/
Newer saw the player who was as last in turn order as a winner until we modified the rules. Now we play this way that the second player takes a bonus 1 coin at the beginning, the third takes 2 coins etc. It neutralizes the cost of taking the card from the board and gives you some chcnces.

RATING: 9
Played it wrong the first time, but overall the gameplay was surprisingly pleasant.

Reasons why it is better than Through the Ages

#1 Player actions are spread out and it isn't one massive turn (speeds up the gameplay and significantly reduces downtime)

#2 Simplification - Not having to pay for buildings, much simpler war/raid rules, moving gold into civil actions (allows much more flexible turns since you can save these up from previous rounds), removing military actions.  

#3 Newbie friendly - The "Growth" step allows people to recover from mistakes by getting the gold/food/ore that they might not have planned for previously.

Reasons why I still might play Through the Ages

#1 Less player interaction.  You can't directly attack players (this could in some ways also be seen as a plus)

#2 You will get to see all of the cards.  We had a significant issue where two players were effectively stuck in the stone age as it came to rock production because there were only two ore producing cards that came up, both which were taken by a single player.  In TtA, you can at least anticipate and know the # of items that will come up for each player

#3 Newbification - There is still something to be said about playing an epic game of TtA with hardcore boardgamers and being absolutely cutthroat.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Gran juego de civilizaciones, con mecánicas de eurogame, eso si.
Tengo que probarlo más antes de dar un veredicto definitivo, pero por el momento me ha gustado.

Se parece al TTA en las cosas buenas y es mucho más ágil porque no tienes que esperar a que los otros jugadores acaben su turno antes de hacer tú el tuyo. Se van alternando las acciones y eso lo hace mucho más dinámico.

Escala bien, te mantiene siempre interesado en la partida y rezuma civilizaciones aunque no es un temático ni mucho menos.

La variedad de cartas, y que no se jueguen siempre todas, hace que las partidas sean siempre diferentes. También ayuda a esto los eventos que van apareciendo que tb. son diferentes siempre y de dureza muy variable.

Lo peor de todo es la caja, que es enorme para lo que contiene. Demasiado aire.

Veremos como va evolucionando, pq superar a TTA no es cosa fácil. Puede que este Nations, al ser tan pulido y tan redondo canse un poco a la larga, mientras que TTA con sus impurezas siempre es un valor seguro. Es una impresión que me ha dado. El TTA no es perfecto, pero tiene algo que hace que sea muy difícil de quemar. No sé si en este caso ocurrirá lo mismo.

RATING: 9.8
Juegazo. Me apetece siempre y creo que es el juego al que más he jugado si no de los que más lo he hecho.

Es un placer evolucionar la civilización.
Cinco variables muy intrincadas entre sí son los engranajes del juego: El dinero (nos permite comprar las cartas y garantizo problemas si no lo tienes), la piedra (nos permite emplazar trabajadores e incluso construir maravillas; aquí es dónde se reparten los puntos, de hecho, al último turno hemos de llegar con piedra como para cambiar las pirámides de sitio), La comida (necesaria para abastecer a la civiliación), y los libros, con una doble función: comodín a falta de abastecer cualquier necesidad y porque se transforman en cinco ocasiones en puntos de victoria, cuatro durante la misma, y una última al contabilizarlos de nuevo y dividirlos entre diez para dar ese número de puntos de nuevo.

Las sensaciones del juego son maravillosas: es evolucionar, es adaptar es crecer en lo militar, es putear con guerra, adelantarte a las necesiadades de los contrincantes, consolidadar la estabilidad, rezar para que las nuevas cartas favorezcan a tus necesidades, cruzar los dedos para que el nuevo evento no te destroce, correr contra los demás por no ser el último en la guerra, o ser el primero en estabilidad o....
y de mientras construir todas aquellas maravillas que ningún juego te ha permitido hacer, luchar con los ejércitos más poderosos y olvidados ludicamente, ser liderados por personajes no mediaticos, librar batallas decisivas que ningún juego reconoce y superar eventos desde la peste negra a las invasiones ándalas,  sucesos que marcaron el devenir y que han sido olvidados  mientras tus obreros desestabilizan la civilización, pidiendo también más comida pero generando más recursos. Ya digo: una experiencia gloriosa

Pero con un defecto: es decir, el juego es un diez. para lo que yo le pido es perfecto.... pero casi. Y el casi viene por el fuerte componente de azar. Casi no existe, es imperceptible: pero está a radales, detrás de cada volteo de cartas: Querer ir a militar y se inviable por no poder adquirir la única carta que da poderío militar; o necesitar piedra y ver solo un edificio de la era uno. ni colonias ni edades doradas.... Todo mitigable con batallas y edades de oro pero que a partidas de cuatro jugadores son tan excasas que apenas te permiten arrastrarte por el tablero pididndo una pedra. Un apuñetera piedra...

Más cosas: aquel mito de que las guerras no eran decisivas, que el poderío militar estaba mitigado por la solidez de la estabilidad era tan solo eso: un mito.

En el juego se brilla de distintas maneras pero si quieres ganar, has de ir a poderío militar y por supuesto a libros (cultura). Provocar una guerra y hacer comerse el punto de pérdida más algún recurso no hace gracia. Responder al poderío gastando recursos en colocar trabajodres que respndan a la fuerza de la guerra es un desgaste que no siempre es asumible. 

El juego está tan bien testeado que al final de la partida la victoria se decide por dos tres puntos... y es ahí donde miras tus huecos de colonias, tus maravillas incacabadas o los quecos que ocupan puestos en edicifios industriales.  Si lo sabré yo!

RATING: 7
Had I not played Through the Ages, then I would have found much to admire about this game.  As it is it simplifies some aspects of TTA, handles the war very nicely, introducing a target set by the player declaring war, which can be mitigated if you declare war yourself by setting it at your own strength level.  Our first game suffered from very few early blue cards appearing, restricting possibilities of employing more meeples.  I guess our expectations were set by TTA, whereas there are some minor variations from TTA in Nations (including the fact that only of a selection of the cards will be used, adding to the replayability but also some 'lumpyness').

There lies my concern with this game.  I cannot recall playing a published game which so closely resembles another published game.  Nations is good but it is not TTA, which was a ground breaker and I think superior.  Plagurism is a work that springs to mind.  I could see no reference to TTA as a source/inspiration in the rule book which I think is out of order.  I do however note that TTA's designer Vlaada Chvátil is mentioned in the credits.  So I assume there must have been some tacit approval of its publication from VC, or maybe this just reflects some tension between CGE and CBG?

As a foot note I should add that whilst four of the five players got some satisfaction from the game, the fifth gave up in frustration, tipped his board over and left.  First time that has happened to me in many year's of gaming.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
The rules are vague in some issues. Have some errors, typos, even on the player board there's a typo, come on, this is not acceptable nowadays.

Lots of things not explained in the rule book (mostly how the cards work in certain situations) fortunately the designers have na excelent FAQ and errata posted here. Also there's some good players aid and guides. So, that should not be a detriment to buying the game, though the price might, for what you get this is a bit overpriced. The player boards are thin and they're not even cardboard. 

Feels like it was meant to be a 2 to 4 player game, but they stretched it to 5 players. Things like "the most" becomes "the 2 most" and the same for least.

The art work is nothing special but is functional.

The game play is very good, very simple, you basically buy cards, from a central space. Those cards can be building, army, wonders, leaders... and then allocate workers to certain buildings and armies to build your resources and knowledge.

The solitaire variant works and is very simple to use. It's a good way to fine-tune your game skills.

RATING: 8
Ez volt a 700-dik játék, amit próbáltam:) Örülök, hogy egy ilyen szintű játékkal jubiláltam. (javítás: elnéztem, csak a 695-dik, nem a 700-dik...) Szinte mindenki a TTA-hoz hasonlítja, nem véletlenül, rengeteg elem visszaköszön onnan, pl. csodaépítés szintjei, kártyatípusok, kártyakezelés (bár az Szentpétervárra is hajaz), erőforrás kezelés (folyamatos aktualizálgatás a pontozótáblán), népesség kajaszükséglete... de valahogy itt minden jóval egyszerűbb, úgy összességében kevésbé mély a játék. Vannak új ötletek is, és a sok hasonlóság ellenére se azt éreztem, hogy egy TTA klónnal játszom (Kevésbé hasonlók, mint mondjuk a Caylus meg a Waterdeep).
Van pár új és jó dolog benne (pl a csodáknál az építész), de szerintem a fő erénye a változatosság: már az alap pakliban is rengeteg a kártya, némelyik egész ötletes, a Nemzet táblák B oldala is új színt vihet a játékba, ezen kívül ott a rengeteg eseménykártya is. Szép, jól működik, de (és itt jön a nagy hibája) nekünk annyira hosszú volt, hogy ez megkérdőjelezi nálam, miért játsszam ezt a TTA helyett. Ráadásul a játék 1/3-ánál tutira látszott, ki fog nyerni, azért egy 5 órás partinál ez kicsit gáz. Ennek ellenére is azt mondom, nagyon jó játék, csak hát civilizációs játékokban a TTA magasra tette a mércét.
Ja még egy dolog, ami fura volt, és többünknek is zavaró: hogy a 4 erőforrás közül 3-at tokenekben tartunk nyilván, egyet viszont a pontozótáblán. A VP-t viszont megintcsak tokenekben, ugyanolyan címletekben, mint a 3 tokenes erőforrást... Nyilván ez nem véletlen, a könyvekre sok kártya hivatkozik, jó hogy nem kell mindig számolgatni, de akkor is fura kicsit:)

RATING: 7
Nations is like the young boxeur entering the ring to contest the title to the champion, and the champion is Through the Ages.
It loses because it's inevitable, but Nations is able to score some points.

1) Nations is definitely shorter, easier to teach and less fiddly than TTA.

2) Wars are handled in a very elegant way. They lack the tension of wars in TTA, but are less punitive to the losers, and military light civilizations won't be abused in every way by stronger opponents.

There are many other points in which Nations is weaker than TTA, though:

1) Less micro-management means more focus in card drafting. It means a lighter game, but also less satisfaction: there is less room to specialize (things have to be fully paid anyway, there is no discounts for upgrading) and strategize (especially considering that the cards would not all come out each game).

2) Scoring is more linear and euro-ish in Nations, while TTA can have wild scoring swings towards the end. TTA has also more card combos. The scoring in TTA grows exponentially, and that means epic comebacks and a stronger story arc.

Overall, Nations is a very good game, and it only suffers from the comparison to a masterpiece. Its lighter fare (it's far to the mind-taxing experience of a game of TTA), easily teachable nature make it a very different beast than TTA, and they both deserve a place in a collection.

I am definitely looking to play it again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
It's a game that keeps you coming back for more. The cards and player powers are varied enough to make every game feel somewhat different--though you're trying to solve a similar tactical puzzle. Nations plays reasonably well at a number of player counts and isn't too hard for new players to pick up and play. The mechanisms of the game allow for the players to perform their moves quickly without adding much downtime for the other players, but certain players could slow the game down to a crawl as they agonize their next move. 

Nations feels more akin to a conventional, tactics-heavy, Euro-style game than a strategy-heavy civilization game. The wars in the multiplayer game appear to be Nations' key contribution to feeling at all like a civilization game outside of thematic art and card design. The solo game feels more akin to a pure euro than a true civ game. 

As for my least favorite aspect of the game: the last round feels somewhat flat. Instead of feeling like you're finally pushing your civilization to its completion, you instead focus on shuffling around your resources to maximize points. It's the least fulfilling moment in the game. The earlier rounds are engaging and fun, however.

In the end, it's a very good, engaging game with some flaws. The anti-climatic final round moves this rating from a strong 8 to a 7.5.

Promos/Expansions/Extras:
Nations: Dynasties (2015)
Nations: Hagia Sophia promo card (2013)
Nations: Nicola Tesla promo card (2013)

RATING: 8
Tried Through the Ages, Civilization, Historia and Nations, still need to try Clash of Cultures.  Nations is the best so far, but not without its flaws.  The excellent solo mode in Nations is a clear advantage, and will surely lock Nations place in my collection for a long time.  Unfortunately, Nations multiplayer is not nearly as friendly a game as it was promoted to be.  As with so many other games in this genre, it is easily possible to get stuck in a downward spiral of death where you are defeated early in the game with no hope of recovery and hours of play still to go.  Aggressive play is also heavily incentivized.  The Dynasties expansion mitigates this somewhat, but not enough to make multiplayer an appealing option again.  It is almost certain that one or more players will not have an enjoyable experience.  Solo mode gets a rating of 10, multiplayer maybe a 6, so 8 overall.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First Play: I held off buying this at Essen because of the frankly amateur artwork. In places it reminds me of the drawings that came with early D'n'D modules, especially the figures. I know it should be about the game but somehow this offended me.

However when offered a chance to play I was happy to accept. It is in essence a simplified version of Through the Ages which I think is a sublime game of balance and skill. However I must say I am suprised at the extreme praise Nations has received this far because it is no where near as good. My main gripe has to be the degree of randomness that exists. In TtA the card strip gives you an indication of whats coming, in Nations a small proportion of the cards are used for each phase so you don't know whats coming or the distribution. In our game only one Military card came out for the first 3 turns and the owner dominated the warfare aspects of the game and turn order. 

It seems that trying to pursue a strategy is a lottery. Just because you need a building that produces more stone doesn't mean one will turn up, meanwhile the turn order can be crucial for one array of cards, irrelevant for another where the cards are middle of the road. There is no way to predict whats coming, you just need crossed fingers and a rabbits foot.

There are so many situations where the luck of the cards can fall in your lap or slap you in the face. The event card is turned AFTER you select whether to take a man or resources so you cant try to manage a situation out, you just have to guess. On one turn for me I took a new man needing food when the event card then gave bonuses to the player with most food, and the cards turned didn't justify an extra man. So often success just comes down to blind luck.

I am confused and somewhat disappointed that so many in the community rate this so highly. If you enjoy the buzz of a luck based game then you will probably like this. Just don't ask me to play with you.

RATING: 10
1. Intro: I confirm that this game is Through the Ages Elegant. This is the best Civilization game I have played and is far better than Through the Ages (43 plays of TTA). This game will rise to TOP 10 and overtake Through the Ages. I also predict that it will also overtake Twilight Struggle and become the number 1 game on BGG because it is that good
2. Weight: Heavy
3. Audience: This game would be a gate way game from medium into heavy euro game. 
4. Players: For 1 to 5 players. Plays best with 5 players imho.
5. Solitaire: Yes
6. Bits: The pieces are good quality. The cards are good stock. 
7. Rules: The rules are concise, clear and well written. If you are a moderator for the game its easy to explain in about 20 minutes.
8. Setup: 10-15 minutes
9. Game-flow: There are eight turns in the game. There is practically no down time. The flow is fast because there are only 3 player actions, The choices in those actions can cause you to pause but there is plenty of time to figure out what to do while others are playing their turns.
10. Randomness: Open card drafting based on turn order.
11. Interaction: The player interaction is well balanced. Choices will deny others cards and there is some confrontation with wars. The wars however can be avoided or the effects can be minimized. 
12. Downtime: Next to know down time.
13. Fun: This game leaves you wanting to play again right away because there are so many different paths to victory. Lots of tension right to the end. Victory point distribution will be close which adds to the end game climax of who is going to win.
14. Theme: There is a story to the game which is your empire and how you built it. I felt ownership of my empire and I definitely had to struggle to manage six different resources
15. Recommendation: I recommend this game for those that love civ games, heavy euros or for those players that want to advance from medium weight games to an elegant engaging short civ game. This game is much better than through the ages because it is less cumbersome that TTA and plays in half the time. If I had to chose between Nations and Through the Ages I would play Nations because it is faster and just as engaging.

PS: I have played Nations 6 times (on the A side and with the Advance cards added) with some very tough opponents and the game shines even more. In addition the game was played an additional 4 times by other members in are group. I bought the game the week before Christmas so this game is very hot. In fact it is hotter than any games I have seen our games group play since I have been a member (2008).  My group has been together since 1975. 

PREDICTION: This is the best Civilization game I have played and is far better than Through the Ages (43 plays of TTA). This game will rise to TOP 10 and overtake Through the Ages. I also predict that it will also overtake Twilight Struggle and become the number 1 game on BGG because it is that good

I recommend that you watch this video:
http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fijbN6sLeEg

Cheers and enjoy

RATING: 6
On its own Nations is decent.
The problem is, that is heavily borrows idears from Throught the Ages and TTA is just much much better.
Many of the mechanisms in Nations are obviously meant to mitigate subjective problems with TTA (long play time, destructive capabilities of wars, exponential point increase).
These are the problems, that make it so hard to get into TTA. The designers of Nations tried to mitigate these. Unfortunately I don't want to play an engine buidling game with only linear resources and points increase. I want a game were the constant risk of war is a real threat and not a minor inconvenience. The shorter playtime is an improvement, but practically worthless, since Nations (similar to TTA) is too long to play it on a weekday and if I play a game like this on a holiday, or weekend, I prefer TTA.

RATING: 4
Nations is so boring and dry that even its supposed «light civilization game» part is merely pasted on a simpler set of rules: in the end you never feel like you’re building a civilization; instead, all you can do over and over until you’re bored to death is bookeeping your resources production and usage.

From the first to the last turn you only keep buying cards (whose generic and uninspired drawings make them indistinguishable from a bunch of similar cards) and grind victory points: you’re not even given the chance of messing with the other players, apart from starting a single war per turn with little to no consequences for your opponents.

This smells (and plays) like a badly-disguised Eurogame, not a civilization game.

RATING: 8
Very interesting game, very much related to "Through the Ages." Veterans of TtA will recognize much here and many of the same considerations. Instead of fiddling with little teeny bits to track items, you are juggling chips of differing sizes and shapes. Someone has contributed a file to let you track your resources on a sliding scale which may make the accounting a little more transparent but it all comes down to personal preference. The cards that represent resource sources, leaders, wonders, and one time injections of resources all work very much the same. Here, instead of "happiness" one tracks "stability" so the similarities continue. This game features a set of event cards that can effect some or all of the players differently each turn and; of course, purchase cards that get refreshed each round that have differing costs depending on which row of the display they end up on.

The biggest difference I can see is the way player turns are managed. In TtA, you do everything you can playing cards and performing actions until you can't do any more then the next player does the same. Here however, each player takes a turn performing one action in rotation until players start to pass, with the round ending when all have passed. This "impulse action" scheme has become much the norm in modern designs and has the appearance of reducing downtime. The games will last just as long but players are "doing something" more continuously instead of waiting for other players to finish taking all their actions. The player mats have options for playing with generic factions (all the same) or using the tailored sides that have different characteristics that would play slightly differently which is a new wrinkle over TtA. 

The key question for me that I can't yet comment on is how differently the two games play. From my first play, it feels like the decisions one is making are just the same as in TtA in getting a functioning economy going and gradually improving it all aimed at garnering the maximum VPs at the end. If anything, this game has even more moving parts and is a very far, far cry from being a "TI-Lite." Frankly, at this stage, I can see no reason to buy this if you already have TtA. While quite similar, I don't see Nations as a big improvement - in fact maybe just the opposite, hence falling into the "if it ain't broke, don't fix it!" category for me. I have always regarded TtA very highly and Nations isn't different, compelling, or better enough for me to switch my allegiance from one to the other. It's not a bad game and I can see myself playing it but not enthusiastic enough to want to buy it.

RATING: 4
I like the variety provided by the huge stack of cards they give you even though, as I'll get to later, the effects of the cards are mostly similar.  I also actually like the art style which has a rough watercolor quality, though I know many don't like it.  The game itself feels very solid and tight, with some choices of what strategy to pursue.  However you can't predict what cards will come up so the game ends up being almost entirely tactical.  You are just buying new cards and covering up older weaker ones and managing several currencies.  The turns all feel the same the numbers just get bigger on your cards and there is no real arc to the game.  It doesn't feel like you're making progress or building something.  The effects of the cards and the resources themselves are very abstract.  Most cards could have pretty much any name.  Battles, wars, and golden ages (essentially discoveries) are especially interchangeable.  I don't think this is particularly bad if you just think of it as a long tactical euro.  But I'm disappointed that the theme doesn't come through more.

RATING: 9
For a game that visually is not that impressive this was quite interesting. I'm still a bit worried about the high amount of randomness in it, but I think on subsequent plays I will be able to combat it with a better strategy.

It will also take a while to see which aspects you can ignore, and which you should prioritize in each situation. I tried completely ignoring military in my first game, and it didn't work. You need it for at least a couple of colonies, otherwise they're just wasted slots.

---

I played it a few more times, and I now see how ignoring military could be a viable strategy, but you'd maybe have to ignore a few more things in order to make it work. If you don't want to buy wars it means you should probably forget about earning VPs that you could lose because of them, so no Golden Ages, and no focus on books. You'd also need enough balance to offset resource loss from wars.

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This game has been compared to Through the Ages, often more favorably, as TtA is accused of being "fiddly." Well, in honesty the fiddly aspects of TtA are what make it great and distinctive. TtA might also be considered more of a gamer's game, as warring is much more brutal, there are fewer catch-up mechanisms, and you know you'll see every card in the game, allowing (theoretically) for more strategic play.

Now, I love TtA, but I must say I prefer Nations. Nations is great because it is not TtA. The card variety is lovely. Yes, it means that some games you'll see very few buildings, and some you'll see nothing but. This is a feature, not a detriment. The game scales incredibly well - I've played with almost every count (not 4, maybe), most often with 2 and with 5, and it's great at every number. The system is more flexible than TtA, in that you can get in and out of certain buildings without having to have foresight or feeling like you've burned a lot of time and resources for naught, but at the same time, good play will carry the day. It has a handicap system that actually works well. VPs are tight, as are resources. War is still powerful, and will generate rivalries, but it is not a game killer, and the damage can be mitigated. Getting new workers is key, but can be difficult; at the same time, it is possible to win without unlocking many of them. To do well, you must be flexible and understand that this is a tactical game, and that you are really playing era by era, rather than for the overall game span (don't ignore books, though, as I guarantee you'll get your lunch eaten). 

The real test is - does it hold my attention? Am I intensely immersed? Yes, every time I play, the outside world recedes. It is a very competitive, cut-throat game, but the mechanisms are simple and recognizable to anyone who plays Euros. No hype here, this is a straight-up masterpiece, and my favorite Civ game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Good game, but this is no Through the Ages. All of the things that made TTA truly epic and stressful--the attack cards, seeding the event deck, setting up the final scoring, worrying about the corruption of resources and the happiness of your populace--are absent here. However, what you are left with is a still a fun, shorter, and significantly lighter game that scratches almost exactly the same itch as TTA. The art is, unfortunately, still pretty bad, and the graphic design still has a lot to be desired, but it's a good game. 

My biggest complaint is that the "books" track is severely underpowered in a two-player game. In fact, unless you are playing a 4-5 player game, there is little incentive to go strongly for books if you will only score 2-3 points per era with the majority. I'd like to see an expansion or an official 2-player rule variant that better incentivizes the book track.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Oh, this is a million times better than Through the Ages. It avoids so many issues with that game, and handles war so well. This is the civ game I wanted TtA to be.

RATING: 10
A great engine building game of juggling several resources.  You're seemingly always short on something, and trying to not any of the balls drop as you manage everything.  Just when you think you have things managed, someone will start a war or an event will come up that makes things tough again!

I love this sort of fight in a game, where I feel like I'm just on the edge of screwing up royally, so I really enjoyed the game play.

I extra super love that the game comes with tons of cards, so you only use a fairly small portion of them from game to game.  It adds a ton of variety from game to game, and I like games where players have to respond to what is available, rather than knowing exactly what will come.  While you can try to go in with an overall strategy in mind, you probably won't be able to follow it exactly due to necessary tactical decisions of making the best of the available options, and responding to the actions of other players and the events.

Overall, simply awesome stuff!  It will draw the obvious comparisons to Through the Ages, and even thought I really like TtA, this game likely replaces it for me due to the more logical nature of it that makes it easier to teach, explain, and play, while keeping all of the tough decisions and tension.

RATING: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.25
It’s inevitable to compare Nations to Through the Ages. They are both quite long civilization games without a map, and you buy cards from a common pool each round and place workers on (some of) these cards. A lot of people who love TtA don’t like Nations as they find it too simple, too short, too luck-based, not enough tension or elements of warfare etc. However, to me, these so-called “cons” are all “pros” in my book! Nations is a much more streamlined civilization game which is a hundred times easier to teach and play. It plays shorter, scales better and doesn’t really force you into a specific strategy (here I’m talking about the fact that if you neglect military completely, you will get hammered in TtA – this is not the case in Nations and it’s a huge improvement). Furthermore, in TtA, you can target a war specifically against one player, whereas in Nations everybody who hasn’t enough military lose a point and perhaps some resources, but not in the same devastating amount as in TtA. Yes, perhaps I’m exaggerating a little, but I really feel that the element of war is condensed and improved a lot in Nations.

This might sound as if I don’t enjoy TtA, but I do. I’ve rated it a 7.5 which is a nice rating compared to my average of 5.9... It’s just too long and too much focused on warfare, so that’s mainly why I prefer Nations - by far. Sure, Nations also has its flaws. The random way in which the cards come out and the fact that a bunch of cards never show up means that you might not get the cards you had been hoping for. In TtA, you know that each and every single card show up at some point, so you can plan for this and use a long-term strategy. In Nations, you need to adapt and make some short-term tactical moves, and while it can really suck that no colonies show up if you have a big military, it’s honestly rarely the case. But, granted, it is a random element which I’m not very fond of. The events each turn are also random, and it happen from time to time that you get screwed because for instance the turn order doesn’t change or the guy with most stability gets a point right after you’ve pulled an extra worker off the stability worker pool… 

Speaking of stability, I think it’s a clever game mechanic that you can mitigate the consequences of war if you have a stable civilization and go down the more peaceful route (and the fact that every player can start the only war each round means that a player with no military can prevent a military defeat if they buy the war first)! The player who has invested more into military will get a reward in terms of going first each turn, probably getting a reward from the events, not losing VPs to lost wars, get income from colonies and resources from battles. But the downside is that this player doesn’t get income from buildings, so the peaceful nations will get a good engine going. It’s a nice balance, and the great thing about the strategies is that you can always adapt and change! You’re not locked into a specific strategy if you chose that at the beginning of the game. Oh, and after more than 10 plays I feel that I can safely claim that military is not an overpowered strategy nor is any other strategy.

Another huge plus is that the elegance and simplicity of Nations. The game consists of 8 turns, and in each of those the players take one action out of three possible actions: buy a card, place a worker on a building/card, or grab an architect to help building your wonder… Yes, it’s that simple! But even so, you still need to think a lot about your decisions each turn (not that it leads to AP in my experience). The resources are also streamlined: you need food for some events, famine and to feed extra workers, you need gold to buy cards and you need stone to place workers on your buildings… Talk about elegant mechanics! There are also the books, which can net you between 0 and 16 points (or 8 in a 3 player game). That’s quite a bit, but again, you sacrifice other resources, so you always need to make a cost-benefit analysis. 
Of course there is some upkeep afterwards and some cleanup and such, but that doesn’t take too long each turn. Military and stability is also handled in a smart way as you instantly move the tracks up and down once you place/remove workers on cards. Clever.

Finally, it’s just awesome that you can play as different nations and on different difficulty levels - it’s a huge improvement on TtA and Clash of Cultures and other civ games! You have some unique powers as the civilization you choose, and they feel somewhat thematic (though the civs can quickly develop into non-thematic civs when you build hoplites in China and build the Pyramids in Greece etc.) I really love that you can choose your own difficulty level so experienced players don’t have too much of an advantage. I’ll have to say, though, that the experienced player still will win most of the games even when playing on the tough difficulty levels like king and emperor; the key is that if the events go their way, they can just take a worker instead of 1-2 resources each turn (as opposed to 3-4 resources on the easier difficulty levels), and then the so-called disadvantage is cancelled and cease to exist.

Judging by the components you get, the price tag for this game is kind of steep. But in my opinion it’s the gameplay you pay for, and I think it’s a reasonable price when you consider this, as it’s such a great game! It should also be quite easy to make an expansion, and I’m fairly sure one will be published eventually.
My final thoughts on Nations are that it works better with 4 players than with 2, but it does work very well across the different number of players. 2, 3 and 4 all work well; I haven’t tried the solo variant yet, but that’s mainly because I don’t play many solo games (Agricola, my number one game, being the only one). It’s a long-ish game with 4, but it’s not too bad as the game takes about 30-40 minutes per player!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I had been eyeballing Nations... not to buy it, but simply to play it. Comments identifying it as Through the Ages 2.0 perked my interest, as I liked a good portion of TtA, but some of its drawbacks ultimately left me not wanting to play it any more.

Compared to TtA, Nations is quicker, more streamlined, less punishing, and possibly a bit more random. There's still the card drafting to build your empire, with buildings, leaders, wonders, wars, etc. It all fits very well together, and I enjoyed my one play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Vladda called - he wants his game back! Seriously though, think of this as a stripped-down Through the Ages and you're most of the way there. There are four rounds/ages...you buy cards from a central row which represent technologies, buildings and wars...you build wonders (and these do not require workers). The list goes on.

Before I start sounding too negative though, this is a good game if you thought Vladda's TTA was too aggressive, or took a bit too long. It's a more streamlined, efficiency-euro type experience. You also don't have to wait for the other players to do ALL their actions before you get a chance - it's one action (buy a card, place a worker on a building, or costruct a wonder), then onto the next player. A very welcome change.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Not a TTA clone, exactly, but it's indisputably enough of an effort to address the same basic ideas that intellectual honesty pretty well demands a head to head comparison. 

So, for the record: it does some things well that TTA does not. For one thing, it's considerably pacier -- a table full of experienced hands (especially not cursed with AP) could crack out even a 4-5 player game in three hours, I suspect. It actually manages to mine the entire history of the world for some genuinely interesting color which makes the game feel a little less like an optimization puzzle with a handwavey history-of-the-word theme. The production is fine; the iconography is clear, although the text is annoyingly small, and I actually thought the artwork (which some people loathe for some reason) is charming. The game overall is more straightforward and less fiddly than TTA.

(A minor point, but one worth noting, is that Nations also makes a more concerted effort to be more global in its scope, incorporating historical figures and other elements from Asia, Africa and the pre-Columbian Americas, as opposed to the relentless Eurocentrism of TTA; this is subtle enough that I'm reluctant to even bring it up lest I froth up the sort of people who complain about PC TYPES RUINING EVERYTHING by committing the sin of pointing out that, say, Korea has a history. But, y'know, the hell with those people.)

That said, my initial effort at the game was a slog that left me unengaged. Progress is slow and incremental for the most part and early mistakes can leave you gasping for air without hope of relief. The greater variety of cards gives the game color and a sort of replayability but it can also leave you in a situation where you just can't hope to collect the resources you need for the strategic direction you planned for early on; in a more fluid tableau-builder like Pax Porfiriana you can pull your opponents back with a clever inversion of the strategic situation, and in a game like Race For The Galaxy, it's over before you get to the point where you feel like a dead man walking. In TTA you know that what you need is EVENTUALLY coming down the pike. In Nations you sometimes need to just sit back and take it. For two hours or more, that's just a bridge too far for me.

I'm trying to keep an open mind on this title; I've been pleasantly surprised by tableau builders in the past, after further reflection. But my initial experience of this game has me longing to play something with a little more player-interaction meat on its bones.

RATING: 7
Better than I expected. Not as deep as Through The Ages, but flows 10x better. Turns are fast, simple. But there is a LOT lost in the way cards flow down from expensive to cheap. It seems a little weird that cards that cost both 1 and 2 bucks all come in and go out together. Workers have a huge upkeep cost. Given that, colonies and wonders (which don't require workers) are even more valuable than they might appear at first. Since colonies require a minimum military force, you really don't have a viable option to ignore military. Sure, you can mitigate the effects of war with stability, but there is still no substitute for getting early colonies. EVen though I think TTA is clearly the better game, I'm actually almost never in the mood to slog through it anymore, so Nations may be a reasonable alternative..

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Interesting and satisfying, but probably too long for its own good. I guess it's Terraforming Mars case which has exactly the same problem. Besides that, it's a good game, surprisingly quite thematic as you actually feel that your civilization grows. Military and stabilisation tracks are worth noticing, as they indiciate your possibilities to conquest various colonies and determine the amount of losses you take when losing a war, for example. War cards are very interesting on their own as they add a nice dose of negative interaction which is just enough for that type of game - and it's indirect, thing I guess is good for an euro game. That type of interaction forces you to change plans rather than striking you directly. Events may be a little bit unforgiving and punishing, but sometimes rewarding as well(for some players). They add a thematic flavour and are mechanically excellent too, because they force players to race in certain fields(resources, military, stabilisation etc.). At first look the game could feel like multiplayer solitaire, but thanks to the mechanisms mentioned above it really isn't.

What I like about Nations is that the game flows smoothly and is kinda light, despite there are several connections between various things, tracks and cards. The rules are simple and straightforward, as in most of the cases it's buy a card or send a worker to a card. Of course there is resource management, creating interactions between cards, keeping tracks of military and stabilisation and so on, but overall it's not really brainburning. However, after 3 hours it gets a little bit tiring and players just want the game to end already. 

All in all, I'm not really sure about the rating, as I feel it could be 8. It's really a nice civilization/engine building game with historical flavour. Adding assymetry between factions could enhance the positive feelings even more - for me it was only the first game so we just played with the same starting cards and resources. I definitely want to try it again, but I guess it's a game to play once in 2-3 months, not more often.

RATING: 5
This has pretty good potential to be an entertaining game, and in fact, I enjoyed the first half of it. The problem I encountered was that it is very difficult to pull out of a losing position. I was living hand-to-mouth, and despite taking the Battles cards to get resources, I was getting knocked down again and again from the events. Will have to play more to see if this is just a result of incredibly poor play on my part, but I have my doubts. 

In Through the Ages, it feels easier to overcome a deficiency in a particular resource. Since the Battles cards are tied to one's military, if you aren't strong, then you won't benefit as much from them. Plus, you might not even get to take the card you need, as you will be going last in the turn. And then you will get smacked around by the events...

I played the Persia B side (chosen randomly), and their bonus is an extra space for a colony. Thinking to capitalize on this, I focused on getting extra workers and building military. However, the starting tech (Axemen) provides only 2 military, so I would need 3 workers on there to colonize even the weakest colony. By the time I was strong enough, other players (whose military techs were worth 3 military each) beat me to the colony cards. Meanwhile, I have these military units who are eating up my resources, and extra population eating up my food, and you can see the downward spiral... And the colonies only get stronger! Agh!

Update: Second play, and this time with beginner cards and Side A boards, and the result was much better management and balance. I still find the game to be very reactionary, rather than developing a strategy and letting it run for a bit. The severity of the events means players have their turn pretty much mapped out for them, and it just comes down to what cards to take to reach that short-term goal. 

Update 2: Third play in, and I'd rather not play this game again. Just doesn't do it for me.

RATING: 8
Nations is a very clean, tight euro strategy game.  Though there are plenty of other games out there like it, Nations does a very good job of making the standard euro style empire building game a very clean but also challenging experience.  The game has plenty of depth to it considering all the options the players will have to choose from.  And knowing every move will be vital to your success.  Like i said, a very 'tight' game.  An veteran should never lose to a first time player, but a match between two experienced foes is always a nail biter.  All in all a game that may not be as unique as others, but gets a very high grade for it's design and execution.

Either way, other than that minor complaint this was a really fun game that is easy to understand once you 'get over the hump'

RATING: 8
[2015-07-09 Rating: n.a. -> 8]
Nations is heavily inspired by Through the Ages (TTA), a game that I really like but that never hits the table. The reasons for this are manifold: There are a lot of (sometimes fiddly) rules, TTA is quite heavy with a lot to learn strategy-wise and a game of TTA is a real time investment. For TTA to shine, you really need to play it over and over again. But since I can't find people that share my interest in TTA, all it does is collecting dust. 

So when I heard of Nations, marketed as a streamlined version of TTA, I had high hopes. For the most part, my expectations have been fulfilled: In contrast to TTA, explaining Nations is a breeze. After one round, everyone should be on board. Nations is not difficult to understand and most of the fiddly parts of TTA have been removed. Especially the "you take only one action then it's the next player's turn" was a great design choice, it simply doesn't take ages until it's your turn again. And like in TTA, it's a joy to balance all the different resources in the game. It's just a very satisfying ride from start to finish.

So, why didn't I rate it higher than TTA? What Nations lacks is the spark of genius (in game design) that TTA has and that I hugely admire and enjoy whenever I get the chance to play it. There are so many ingenious ideas in TTA, such as the seeding of the event deck, that I felt I had to rate Nations a tad lower.

RATING: 8
Players guide an ancient empire through four "ages" of advancement, trying to score the most victory points.

Players on their turn take one of three actions - purchase a card off the drafting board; recruit a worker into an owned building; hire an architect to work on a wonder of the world.  Repeat until all have passed, and do this for 8 turns spread across four ages.

This can be best described as Through The Ages, ported to an action impulse system.  The game play is quite similar - purchase cards, port your population onto them to generate an engine, etc.  However, the mechanics are now take a single action, then pass the turn around the table, rather than wait for every other player to take a complete turn before the action comes back to you.  On top of that, each turn there are two or three mini-competitions taking place with rewards and penalities applied to the nations with the most/least of certain resources.

The sum of this is the game feels faster than Through The Ages, and indeed it may be, but it is still quite a long game, especially with four or five players.  Still, the fast "take 1 action" game play keeps you involved and interested, and there is literally no downtime as you play the game.  

The victory conditions are tighter as well - instead of building up a Through The Ages like culture engine, and slapping down some hidden goal victory points at the end of the game, here you are in a cut-throat stuggle for victory points, which are a rare commodity and each one matters.  A score of 40 may well win the game here.

Through The Ages is a top 10 game for me - I adore it.  This too is an excellent game and may yet take its place.  I am still exploring the basic game - I haven't flipped the boards over to the alternate setup sides, or tried the other two tiers of cards yet.  So, much to explore, but my first three games have been excellent.  High hopes for this one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Kipróbáltam egyedül 3 játékost irányítva. Jól elhúzódott a történet, mert nehéz volt úgy játszani, hogy ne vegyem nagyon figyelembe a különböző játékosaim stratégiáit. Így is sikerült különböző módon fejlesztenem őket, függetlenül attól, hogy direkt a játékostáblák 'A' oldalát használtam. Volt amelyik "aranytermelőre" sikerült, volt amelyik a stabilitást "maxolta ki" a játék végére (habár a haderőre egyáltalán nem koncentrált) és volt amelyik a haderőt preferálta eredményesen. Végül az utóbbi nyert 41-38-37 arányban. Azt olvastam valahol, hogy hasonlít a Saint Petersburg játékhoz. Valóban a fejlődéskártyák tekintetében észre lehet venni némi hasonlóságot, de a játék azért egészen más. Eddig még nem játszottam civilizációépítő játékkal, így ez nekem egy külön élmény volt, ahogy láttam a birodalom fejlődését. Habár én inkább azt vettem észre, hogy egy nagy nyersanyag menedzselés a játék, mert hogyha valamilyen erőforrás hiányba keveredtem, akkor mindig lehetett találni egy épület, csoda, tanácsadó és még ide sorolnám a csata lapot is (habár ahhoz szükség van a haderőre), amivel kisebb megakadás után tovább lehetett fejlődni. A könyveknek nem éreztem akkora szerepét, habár lehet azért nem, mert alapvetően biztonsági játékos vagyok, aki nem szeret kifutni a meglévő nyersanyagokból, pedig szerintem néha megérte volna a kockázatot. A szabályok megértéséhez kétszer kellett elolvasnom a szabálykönyvet, hogy minden teljesen letisztuljon, viszont a segédletet játék közben is nagyon jól lehetett használni és egy játék után úgy érzem, hogy a szabályok egyáltalán nem bonyolultak, amennyiben felfogjuk a stabilitás szerepét, a háború lefolyását, teendőket a nyersanyag hiány esetén és a többi már egészen egyértelmű (ha már több komolyabb társasjáték is van mögötted :)). Talán még a kártyák kavarhatnak be egy keveset, de ebben is segít a hivatalosan kiadott FAQ. Ráadásul a magyar szabályt már az Errata-val is kibővítették, így azt teljesen feleslegesen olvastam el külön is :). A szabály kitér az első játék alkalmával betartandó ajánlásokra, amiből szerintem csak a saját játéktáblák 'A' oldalának használatát érdemes figyelembe venni. A kártyák meg hadd biztosítsanak változatosabb fejlődési lehetőségeket. Emlékszem az Agricola-nál is figyelembe vettem az ajánlást és csak az "egyszerű" pakli lapjait használtuk, amit végül megbántam, mert amíg az egyik játékosnak a 14 lapjából 8-9 is teljesen hasznosnak bizonyult, addig én örültem, hogy végül 4-et ki tudtam játszani, mert a többit nem találtam annyira előnyösnek. Tehát visszatérve a Nations-re, hadd menjenek a kártyák, én nem találtam közöttük egyet sem, ami bármiféle nehézséget okozna, habár azt hozzá kell tenni, hogy körülbelül a negyedét sem láttam :). Többet játszó családoknak bőven ajánlanám, akik nem riadnak vissza a hosszabb játékidejű játékoktól, habár a szabályban erre is van egy ajánlás, miszerint abba lehet hagyni, előre megbeszélés alapján, a harmadik korszak végén is, habár én nem korlátoznám a birodalom teljes kivirágzását :). (2015.08.30.)
Ha egy gondolkodós társaság játszik vele, akkor nagyon el tud húzódni. Nekünk be kellett fejezni a Reneszánsz kor után időszűke miatt, így is négyen több mint 3 órát játszottunk. Ráadásul a szabály magyarázatnál elfelejtettem a háború szabályait részletesen elmagyarázni, így nem volt teljesen tiszta, hogy a fekete háború jelölő nem mozog, ha a háborút kirobbantó játékos hadereje változik, illetve ami még fontosabb, elfelejtettem, hogy a stabilitás csökkenti a háború veszteségit. A játékélmény még mindig kiváló. A hosszú játékidő ellenére egyáltalán nem vettük észre az idő múlását, mert mindenki élvezte a birodalom építését. Érdemes az éhezésre odafigyelni, mert könnyen bajba kerülhet, aki nem számol vele. (2015.08.31.)

RATING: 7
In some ways, a much-improved version of Through the Ages.  Gives a decent civ feel, as you push resources around and collect cards, but still pretty abstract.  It's not as punishing as TtA: there's no way to directly attack someone, and even the wars aren't crushing.  The events can be brutal, however, and being behind on the military or order tracks may result in severe penalties.  Growth is tough, as well, since any citizen that you activate imposes a cost for the rest of the game.  You really have to plan well, since leaving yourself short of rock will mean that you can't activate the new buildings you buy and being broke means that you can't buy cards.  Both can be crippling, so you really don't want to wind up losing a turn that way.
What felt unbalanced to me was just the way that the cards came out onto the display.  There always seems to be at least one war available, so I think there are slightly too many of those (as you only ever need one per turn).  Other crucial cards, like new buildings, military units, battles, etc. may be in short supply.   You may be hurting in a particular area, but if the card display isn't cooperating, you won't be able to fix the problem.  If you want to play at the top level with all the cards, I recommend going through them and separating by type (there are either 8 of the more common types like buildings or 4 of the less common types like wars per tier).  Once that's done, shuffle the types separately, then put the right number from each pile into the deck for that age and shuffle again.  It's time-consuming, but does result in a better distribution than having a massive deck.
Wonders are kind of problematic as well, and I don't know if they're really worth buying a lot of the time.  That's because architects are very scarce, and if the other players decide to fight you for them, you'll never be able to get the thing built in a timely manner.
I don't know if military is too important, but it's certainly not something you can continually ignore.  Not only does it determine turn order, but it's the only way to buy colony cards.  You'll definitely want to get some of those before long, as they're free resource production.  There does seem to be an ebb and flow to the game, though.  It's not always just the same player sitting at the top of the track, since new cards will come out and other players will prioritize being in first place.
Anyway, there's a few oddities, but I kind of like it all the same.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.41
Great medium civilization building simulation similar to TTA but simpler. The core mechanic of the game is the progress card offer from which players buy the card they wish, place them on their sheet and add workers or architects to them. Each card is unique, thematically perfect and historically accurate and there is around 300 of them for 4 different ages. Every age is divided in 2 rounds, each round refreshes card offer and brings new events (which set the mini goals of the round) and every second round there is mini scoring with full blown scoring in the end.
There is also only 3 types of resources and 3 progress scales to maintain which is the simplest it can get for civilization game.
It can be played up to 5 or even solo and it's still fun.
Just brilliant. Good work Finnish designers, again.

[b]Update:[/b] Rating modified according to a custom rating system.

[b]Favorite mechanism:[/b] 3 rows of advancements which you can buy from. 3rd row is the most expensive but it will be the only one to stay for the next round.

RATING: 6
I'm torn on this game as I expected it to be more exciting. There are some intelligent design choices, but the game seems more about developing an efficiency engine and due to tedious bookkeeping & re-checking each round, high card randomness, a feed your people obligation (usually benign), harsh artwork (especially the portraits - yikes!), extremely tight resource management, and a 4th age which drags at the 3 hr mark, I was left somewhat underwhelmed.  Ultimately, the theme never came through anywhere near as strong as I had hoped (all eras more or less play and feel the same) and there is no story arc or narrative.  It's more puzzly than epic and there are some very gamey cards and non-thematic designs for effect:  why can I only have 5 buildings? Why only 1 advisor or 2 colonies at one time?  Why does my military strength go from the best in game down to to zero the instance I develop a military advancement?  Why do wars get progressively more costly while employment costs increase so much?  Why does the parliament produce as much stone as the factory? Many cards are various tweaks on production and by the 4th age, everyone's just scouring the board for maximum victory point generating opportuinties.

I haven't played Golden Age, nor the 4hr Through the Ages to compare yet, but I probably prefer 7 Wonders or Deus instead.

UPDATE:   TTA is a card-based civ builder, but somewhat a different game.  I can see Nations probably easier to get to the table for me, but TTA has a definite narrative and story arc as your empire really grows and the ages feel like progress.

RATING: 7
Through the Ages light. *Much* easier to learn, *much* quicker, but much less elegant, much less engrossing. I never feel as connected to my civilization as I do when playing Through the Ages, and I can never managed to feel imperilled in this game. You can play *extremely* poorly throughout, and then recoup a TON of points at the end from population, wonders, loyalty, etc. (I did exactly this last game I played, and came in a close second to a player who played an almost flawless game). So, not very balanced. Also, there's surprisingly little difference between building options, which makes any particular decision to buy one feel just kind of 'bleh'. There's a "might as well" feeling to most decisions you end up making. Point tallying at game-end is ridiculous. I think this is in part why its hard to develop a connection to what you're building. You don't feel like your building something distinct and customized; you merely feel like you're building what you need to build in order to make the most resources.

It gets points for being a civ game -- they're just fun by default -- and for snagging a bunch of great mechanics from Through the Ages. To the extent that Nations might introduce a few more people to Through the Ages, I applaud it. Otherwise, it's just kind of flat.

RATING: 4
I get what the design was going for in terms of the tension of the card display in TtA, but reducing the game to that one element removes far too much. Furthermore, if you're going to tilt the game this heavily towards the card drafting, then the cards need to be far, far more differentiated so at to provide different paths. A good counter-example is Lewis & Clark that I also recently tried; there, the cards offer very different benefits and ways to go about your business. The cards in Nations were very mild upgrades and differences from previous ones. 

You aren't rewarded for going down any particular path because all new cards must be paid for in full, so even though you have a military unit, that new one will cost you the same it costs a player who has no military. This is a huge diff from TtA, of course, and the result is, ostensibly, the flexibility to buy any card at any time and shift gears easily. The price for that is any sense of investment in your "civilization." 

There still is a heavy emphasis on military here which I thought was what they wanted to nerf from TtA? Military cards got drafted first due to the ability to raid and colonize as well as the ability to stay in first place so you could take more military cards. Raids are very beneficial in game boons, and colonies score at game end without having to be manned. I mean, there's even more of a military slant in this game than TtA. At least let the player last in military go first, for heaven's sake!

RATING: 10
I'm going to say it, this is a far better experience then Through The Ages.  It takes half the time, has half the rules, is far more streamlined and elegant and is far more balanced.  I like the double sided player boards, with the A sides all being the same regardless of which civilisation you choose and the B sides being unique.  I also love the sense of escalation and the feeling that you are really building towards something, without having to rely on any one strategy or fear getting the sh*t slapped out of you.  War and conflict is quite abstract but easily explained and not a dominating factor, just one of a lot of small finely balanced features that fit together like a Swiss watch.

The only complaints that I have mostly relate to the components, which are mediocre at best and poor at worst.  Plus the box is unnecessarily large for what is inside.

Lastly, there can be some variety issues at lower player counts, with a card distribution that may become unbalanced if you are using all of the cards with less than 4 players.  There is a way to solve this: removing the expert cards means more of the deck will be used therefore guarantying a more even spread, although you do potentially rob yourself of some of the more interesting cards.

El mejor juego de mi colección.

RATING: 9.25
6/23/2015-A almost perfect euro. My game groups
favorite  game. dc


My score is 9.2.dc

12/9/2015-I believe I must go into this in more detail. the game begins with every one picking a nation now you and your game group can do one of two things.(1#choose side A and you get one extra building space)(2#choose side B and you each get a special power coruspanding with the nation you select.)the cards are then put out on the card board. Once you finish the rest of setup. You then get to do one of three actions(1#place a man on a building card)(2#undeploy a man off a building card)(3#you may buy a card).Now the most interesting of the choices(in my humble opinion)is number three you may buy one of five different cards, which I will explain in more detail. I will start with military cards. When you a military card you can place it in one of your four  spots so you can deploy one or more of your men on it. Sadly this is all I can write at this moment ,but if your interested watch rado's run through of it on board game greek or you tube. dc

 

The best worker replacement game!xs

RATING: 4
If you're looking for your grand civilization experience in Nations you'll be disappointed. Even though the theme binding is rather well done it is fairly subdued. In the abstract the game is basically a pretty simple engine building game with incremental rather than exponential improvements. You'd think the streamlined rules would make for a quick game then, but for some reason it still takes quite a long time to completion. Too long, in fact, considering that the gameplay is fairly tactical in nature due to the way the cards come out.

RATING: 8
Fun civ builder.  Rather similar to TTA: New Story, but lacking in certain key areas.

Comparison to TTA: New Story
Pros: 
+Variable nation strengths
+Shorter length and somewhat easy to teach
+Number of players
Cons:
-Much more meter-centric = theme feels more haphazard
-More limited spaces = less realism
-Less dramatic tension
-Events/cards sometimes have little thematic connection to what is actually happening in the current game

RATING: 6
There are a few things missing from TTA: The seeding of events, upgrading of your buildings imho more elegant resource management, less strategic more tactical. Also, military feels more restricted here: There is no distinction between different types of military units and no formations/army cards. The other players are not (battles/raids) or almost not(wars) by your war efforts; however military helps you moving up in turn order and gaining bonuses from events.

Nevertheless, Nations feels like 75% of TtA, plays in the same amount of time but is so much easier to teach and learn (learning time approx. 30 mins).

I rate it a 9 overall and I'm keen to try it again, although I'm unsure if I will ever own a copy, since TtA is one of my favourites, we know how to play it and we only play it 3 or 2 players.

RATING: 7
A very good and interesting game with a lot of interesting decisions. The variability of the cards which could come into the game can screw you quite a bit. If f.e. you miss out to get a buildig for more corn in the first age and in the scond and third age no such building appears it can be very difficult stay competetive to the others.

RATING: 6
Easier to teach then TTA and far more forgiving due to built in catch up mechanisms. There is more randomness due to not all cards showing up each game. While this does give more variety, it also makes long term strategies risky, further hindering experienced players from being able to take advantage of their knowledge of the game. Again, this is both good and bad. 

I'll keep TTA, thanks, but I can see the appeal of having a card based civ game that is easier to teach and easier to introduce to new players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Rating after 1/5/10/25 plays: 8/8+/-/-

Yes! Yes! Yes! The Civ game I've been waiting for. 

+ Plays in a reasonable amount of time. (40-60 minutes per player)
+ "Tech track" in the form of Progress cards: Buildings, Military upgrades, colonies, Wonders and Advisors.
+ Solitaire-friendly. No hidden information. Has solitaire rules but can be played solo with two or more competing nations. Solo at Prince using basic cards was Meh. Solo at King difficulty with the basic and advanced. Cards was much more enjoyable (tense)
+ Not too difficult to learn but mastery takes skill and time
+ Theme is interesting but rules and theme are not completely tied together. The mechanisms and progress card effects match the theme on the card (e.g. Marie Antoinette provides lots of "culture" books but you'll have to take a significant hit on Stability). There will be ahistorical situations such as Rome building Pyramids, China taking on Montezuma as an Advisor. It's not difficult to imagine a sci-fi or fantasy re-theme.

Will likely pimp this game with wooden tokens in place of food and stone cardboard counters. Card decks will be shuffled once per game - still on the fence about sleeves.

First impressions:
FUN: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
RULES: :star::star::star::star::star:
ART: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
COMPONENTS: :star::star::star::nostar::nostar:
THEME: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar:
LONGETIVITY: :star::star::star::star::nostar:

Top scores:
1P: King, Base+advanced: 43 China-B. Prince, Base cards: 43 a-Side

RATING: N/A
Played 1 2-player and 2 solitaire games and can not quit thinking about it. One quick note: I've played all three games wrong as I did NOT take a second Stability when placing a second worker on a card giving stability. As a result I have struggled to get any stability going, and when I have to take a Stability hit, it has been devastating (in the solo game). But after rethinking the game I realized that the cards with Square resources do not mean that the gain is only ONCE (immediately after placing your first worker on it), but it is given immediately EACH TIME you place another worker on it. This still would not have helped much as a) I have yet to get a card that gives positive gains for Stability(!).

RATING: 7
Overall, it is a well-constructed civvy game. Nothing new, but all its parts come together nicely. There was something that made me feel like replayability would be an issue. I would try it again, but more likely try it again with 4 players instead of the full 5 (which seemed more like an after-thought in the design). Fortunately, turns went quickly and one could puzzle the purchasing board for options when it wasn't their turn, so you didn't just have to sit there waiting for your turn to come around again. For a complex game, I enjoyed the simple structure. Punishing in places, but not too punishing. Gave me that "chase good money after bad" feeling where you don't know how much to invest in one of the advancement areas before you should just give up or throw all your chips in. That added tension throughout.

RATING: N/A
I ordered it and it arrived 6AUG2015. Nations board game box was damaged, and improperly packaged, so I sent it back to Amazon. I returned it, and Amazon sent a second one which had a good game box (I thought), but they still did not properly pack the inside of the shipping box. When I opened the Nations game to do inventory, one side of the insert was busted. I blame this on the extra big box with a lot of empty space inside it, and the heavy components laying against the insert. However upon further inspection on the external side of that Nations box, it was apparent this game had been mishandled before it ever was put in the shipping container. There was an impact area along the edge of the game box where it had either been improperly stacked, or dropped or something (but the shipping container was in perfect shape externally). I returned this second Nations game to Amazon and canceled my order completely. I received full refund 28AUG2015. The game was purchased from "Newbend Discount". So there you go, I spent a good portion of August attempting to get a good copy of the game. Maybe it just wan't meant to be. I pre-ordered "The Gallerist" from a local game store instead. Maybe Nations will get a better second printing.

RATING: 9
[b]Solo Rating: 8.5[/b]

Recommended player count: 1, 3-4.

2 players is okay but very cut-throat. 

5 players can be okay but game may go on too long.

[b]Replaces:[/b] Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization

[b]Comments:[/b]

Each age presents a new and enjoyable tactical puzzle. You can't make plans much beyond the current round, yet there is a wonderful sense of progression as you build up your nation and move through history. 

If I want to play a civ themed game, I'm either going to play this or "The Golden Ages" if I want something a little lighter (and with a shorter play time).
