RATING: 4
Rating based on several games at mabiweb:
The game is... strange. On one hand it is exactly like TTA - with all these cards etc. On the other - it's completely different: Nations is more streamlined, the resources are... just resources the grain and stone and other things might be something completely different, with cards not really matching the civ-theme. This much more of a economic engine building game with a civ theme on. It's not bad, actually managing all the resources is a little bit tough thing to do and needs a little bit of practice.
I like it, but I won't buy it. If I am to play a civ game I'd rather stay with Innovations (yes, this is more of a pastiche on civ game, but a GREAT one) or TTA. 
It's great to see a game I was wondering if I'd like to buy and finally see I don't need it. :D Maybe it's me staying too long in the hobby? :)

EDIT
After several more plays - this is so much of an exercise in tactics and pure spreadsheet type of thing that I'm fed up with it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A civilization builder that plays out in a reasonable period of time. There's no tech tree mechanism here; instead, nations are purchasing from a common pool of buildings in order to fill a limited set of slots. The constrained choices prevent the decision tree from getting too large, which keeps the game moving.

This is not a game which delivers on the feeling of building up a nation over centuries; it feels much the same in the first era as it does in the last. What it does deliver on is competition for the best buildings and best position on various upgrade tracks.

RATING: 6.5
Solid Civ game (played with Dynasties expansion at least.)

Through the Ages is much, much better because you will see all the building/leaders etc. during a game. This game Nations  uses only a small fraction of the available cards, so there is no ability to plan-- It is all tactical based on the random cards drawn each round to be placed on the purchase board, with little long term strategy possible. You may plan to buy a good resource producing building, but none may be available for several rounds, and there are only 8 rounds in the game. Bleah.

Seems a shame for a civilization development game.

RATING: 7.5
Very fond of this game thus far, I have been playing a ton solo.  Solo game is solid out of the box and if it weren't so expensive I would say it is worth it even to buy it for solo play.

Overall, I think it is the non-conflictual, euro civ champ.  So that means it is second billing to Clash of Cultures, which is best in show for Civ games.  Military is executed perfectly (for what it wants to do; important but not nasty), and the progress feels good, but not great.  I wouldn't call this "epic" because most of the progress is is simply getting bigger numbers for your buildings.  Still, the game manages to induce tension by making sure you'll always be weak in at least one resource.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I was pleasantly surprised by Nations, having already played Through the Ages. What I can say right off the bat is that the often made comment that TtA leans further towards strategy and Nations more towards tactics was wholly my experience. However, since they are cut from very similar cloth, I can make my comments comparative.

Things I liked in comparison: 
- 1. The way in which actions are singular and alternating means that you don't have to wait for someone to grind their way through their turn (and it makes it easier for you to not do so either!).
- 2. The cards not all coming out means that players have to adapt a whole lot more. There's no meta-game involved in waiting out Napoleon here.
- 3. Events add a bit of meta-spice to the mix, making players have to adapt a bit more.
- 4. Variable and asymmetrical starting powers are an option.
- 5. End game scoring couldn't be any easier.

Things I disliked in comparison:
- 1. Wars, what happened to them? First of all, the only type of aggression available are wars and their effects can also be heavily mitigated through the influence track. This potentially means that the game becomes primarily one of drafting cards and optimizing one's position. I understand that aggression is the most difficult thing to balance out within a civilization game -- to brutal and it feels almost pointless to build things up in the first place, too soft and you're barely encouraged to build up a military.

While I still prefer Through the Ages to Nations, I think it is a good design in its own right, even if the inter-player interaction has been nerfed considerably.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
(03/14/2014) Comment based on one play.

This is a great game. I enjoyed my first play so much that I bought it from the owner at a board game meetup!

Within a few turns, I could easily see this is a far more accessible, streamlined, and intuitive game than Through the Ages. I've only played the short game of TTA once, and that was enough. Nations plays smoothly and quickly, while TTA was like sifting through a spreadsheet. At least, it is for me--this is a far superior game; I didn't realize how quickly it had risen through the BGG ranks. I was prepared to be frustrated and bored, because of the similar card drafting mechanism, but the three rows of cards provides plenty of visibility and flexibility, rather than a single row like in TTA. It only took about 15 minutes to explain, which is much shorter than most civ games I've played in the past. 

Cards represent buildings, monuments, bonuses (golden age), advisors (famous historical figures), raids, colonies, wars, and military. They can be bought from one of three rows, costing 1/2/3 coins, where unbought cards from the top expensive row (3 coins) drops down to 1 coin, similar to St. Petersburg. Workers are placed on the building cards to "build and man" the building(s) by spending stone. The building can immediately boost up stability, or increase coin/food/stone/book production. Monuments require architects, which are in limited supply, to complete by spending stone also. Placing a worker on a military card temporarily boosts the military rating of the civilization, but is expensive and has either a one-time cost, or a sustaining cost in terms of food/stability/books. If a military rating is high enough, it is possible to acquire colonies, which usually give you sustained production of a type of resource. Wars can be declared by buying such a card, and then setting the max military level that must be achieved, which is similar in feel to 7 Wonders, or a penalty must be paid. If there is at least one worker on military, it is possible to pick up raid cards, which result in one of the food/stone/book resources. Advisors can give sustaining income of food/stone/book, as well.

In general, coins are needed to buy cards, stone is needed to man the buildings and to get resource production, food is required to feed extra workers, as well as pay a random amount due to famine on the event card, which is different for every one of the 8 turns. Books represents knowledge, and having more is helpful in scoring VP in one of four book scoring rounds. Stability is sort of the opposite of "misery" in Age of Renaissance, and reduces the impact of losing a war. It is fascinating to watch a military build-up, in response to a war card, only to watch disarmament the next round, depending on the game state, as well as the player's objectives.

Yes, the tracks of stability, military, and books seems similar to the various tracks in TTA. Other than that, it feels more like a game of London, with players upgrading their "stacks" of buildings (although you are limited in the number of stacks, and you actually get rid of the older building). Similar to Twilight Imperium 3rd Edition and Eclipse, you can take as many actions as you wish during a particular turn (of eight), provided you have enough resources to do what you want. There are no geographical maps, but there is a player tableau where players can customize their own respective layout.

Although the theme is a bit more abstract, one gets the sense of moving through time and history, at a fairly rapid clip. Starting from ancient times, the game reaches the industrial revolution within eight turns. There is plenty to explore here, as well as enjoy. Components are very good, with sufficient rules. Play is relative quick, with little downtime, and many meaningful decisions each turn. A player can take as many actions as they wish per turn, subject to the limits of resources, so this is not a fixed number of turns type of game. Further, the complexity of the game can be adjusted by taking out or inserting specific cards, and the complexity can be individually adjusted through the amount of resources one gets at the beginning of each turn. Thus, a stronger veteran player can effectively give a handicap to a new player or comparatively weaker player. Initial rating is "9", with room to move up very quickly. This could be the best abstract civ-lite game out there that plays within 2 to 2.5 hours.

(04/06/2014) 4 plays: What is so enjoyable about Nations is watching civilizations build up military, when the event card and/or war provoke it, or seeing a juicy colony. The militaries can then disarm the next turn, so that the workers can be reassigned to "peacetime" activities. The same goes for building up stability, since the event cards and/or war can provoke an increase. As the game progresses through the four ages, the buildings and military types upgrade, giving more production/strength/VP, but at greater cost.

However, the game has a dark side, with a serious case of "runaway loser", where if one is unable to keep up with the punitive events, the game will mercilessly continue to punish the player for the rest of the game; it is literally impossible to catchup--the leaders will continue to steamroll forward in all aspects. Also, Rome's "B" side may be too powerful, since with a Legionnaire and +2 built-in military, with one worker placement, it has first-grab of any Era I colony(s) that pop up; it is then easy to roll over Persia, even though it has 3x colony slots.

(05/18/2014) 16 plays. This is the hottest game in our group. Everyone wants to play it, although there are some mumblings about it starting to feel same-y. The game is starting to scream for more nation tableaus--five is not enough variety. After playing at the Emperor level, all are convinced of the awesome tension that this game delivers. We also played a 5-player for the first time, and this was also a phenomenal experience. With one newbie, the 5P game took about 2 hours 45 minutes, which is much, much less than a comparable game of Through the Ages with four players! The fact that difficulty can be tailored through the handicap, the asymmetrical boards, three progress decks, and three tiers of event cards, this game has a lot of re-playability. The fact that everyone wants to play it again, after two hours, and the fact that we can squeeze two games of this on a game night, speaks volumes to the sheer brilliance of this design. It hits all the right buttons and scratches the civilization itch like no other. I'm upping this to a solid "10".

I have wondered about why a map-less civilization game could be so successful (ranking at #49, as of 5/2014), when compared to [thing=40765][/thing] (ranking at #142)? It seems that Nations is able to effectively convey the feeling of nation building and evolution through time, although many aspects of nation building are highly abstracted. War, for example, is a simple comparison of military strength indexes. There are no spatial elements to worry about; that is, no fronts, flanks, or logistics. CoC is brilliant in that it drills down to the unit level, all which can be moved around on a hex grid board, replete with terrain. CoC also has a superb exploration mechanism that Nation lacks. While CoC is wonderful in that it simulates the growth of individual nation-states, with its five-piece cities, it is essentially stuck in the Antiquity/Classical era, in the time of Homer. Nations speeds through four Ages in a matter of two hours, giving players just enough of a taste of each period, before moving on to the next age. It emphasizes the march of technology, where nations had better be constantly modernizing, or be quickly cast aside into the dust bin of history. It is this sense of urgency, the need to upgrade, to grab that critical progress card, or to trigger an war event, all before someone else does, that gives Nations the wonderful tension and nail-biting decisions. This is further exemplified by the seemingly quick rounds, where players fire off action-by-action, causing a constant ebb and flow of action and reaction. So, you don't really need a map to vividly convey the rise of a civilization. You only need some abstract representations to guide you along. In that, Nation succeeds in spades. The fact that it plays in about two hours is just about the threshold for most gamers I know--it is long enough to develop a story and feel somewhat "epic", but short enough that you don't have to suffer through the occasional bad game. It certainly leaves the feeling of "what if" making the players wanting to play it more in order to experiment. CoC is still a wonderful game, and is spatially very interesting, but it is also correspondingly more sophisticated. By comparison, Nations is not as sophisticated, but it is still very interesting, because it is highly interactive from the beginning. Every action can affect all the other players, and the events will motivate or discourage all players equally; how they respond to (and profit from or mitigate) each event tends to sift the winners from the losers. CoC is more akin to classic wargaming, where there are fronts and flanks and other spatial considerations, and thus is less interactive--you may be focusing all your energy on only one opponent, if there two or three. This is neither good or bad, but simply shows the varied interests and motivations of today's board gaming audience.

See [url=https://www.boardgamegeek.com/plays/thing/126042?userid=149367][b]plays[/b][/url] to read extensively detailed recorded plays, sessions, and strategies.

RATING: 8
A streamlined version of Through the Ages that plays well across a variety of player counts and has a war mechanism that doesn't involve the weakest player being constantly picked on. You always seem to be perpetually short of all the in-game currencies (money, food and minerals) and the early turns feel like a struggle against bankruptcy. Only a small proportion of the ample card decks will come out each game, so it doesn't pay to go too far into one strategy (there's no point in going heavily into military with no battles or wars to fight, for instance), it's better to stay flexible and do a bit of everything. This can make the game feel a little bland, I'm not sure there are really competing strategies, but would definitely play again.

RATING: 9.5
Excellent civilization building game. I've played it at 1, 2, 3, 4 and 5 players. The solo game is honestly not that great, but it works marvelously at the other player counts (beware that a 5-player game is lengthy. On the order of 3 to 4 hours). Haven't played a boring one yet. It has balanced A/B sides for each player board, interesting mechanics for growth, war and player interaction, and excellent production values (with the exception of the default box insert; I've seen worse, but it's not exactly great).

It manages the trick of being a substantial, thinky game that's fairly easy to teach.

RATING: 9
A pleasantly crunchy thinking game where you develop your nation from antiquity to modern era (although this is more abstracted through the purchase of cards).  It is fairly long playing, around 2-3 hours, but is structured to avoid most down time.  Player on player aggression is limited to war cards where you try to run the military track to impose penalties on army weak players (almost always Egypt).  Strategic play/purchase of cards makes it fun, offers a solo play option.  This is the exact type of game I truly enjoy (Civilization without the trading and military eye-gouging).

RATING: 9.22
[b]2 Player Review:[/b]

If only I could create my ideal world of prosperity and wealth; but just like in real life, you have to work with what you've got in this very puzzle-like civilization builder. 

Making a square plug fit in a round hole is what the game felt like on the first few attempts, often ending with massive deficits or just never ever seeing a kind of card I was really banking on, over several turns. But just like most card-driven games, you really are just making all the available pieces fit, your ideal world be damned. Once I let go of this desire to build a full set of wonders, or manage a full loop on the culture track, my enjoyment of the game sky-rocketed. And while it certainly sounds sad to give up on your dreams, there is certainly a lot of fun to be had in the production phase and in the tactile nature of building your civilization. 

[b]Solitaire Review:[/b]

This game is the main culprit for me getting more and more into solitaire board gaming. There is almost no more management required than that in the main game, and all the fun is still completely intact. Even as I write this, I'm thinking of what kind of civilization I could build with what fate the cards present me, and whether I can beat my last high score. 

[i]F.Y.I.[/i] My enjoyment of the game greatly increased after removing all of the advanced cards from the game. While I usually revel in more complex games, here it just adds more randomness to the card draws and more often than not results in a wall of military and war when you just want to see a little of something else. 

[b]Pros:[/b] 

- A great, relatively quick civilization builder, that does feel very epic in scale. 
- The game-play is very stream-lined. Leaving workers on the cards between turns speeds things up considerably. 
- Military doesn't directly hurt or destroy another player's civilization, only hinders point scoring most of the time. 

[b]Cons:[/b]

- the cards that are available can be very frustrating sometimes, especially when there is a wall of military and you're completely focused on production. 
- Loosing on both Military and Stability tracks at once will be extremely frustrating, until you figure out the game-play and realize it's not usually that important, unless the events that turn are brutal. 

[b]2015 Rating:[/b] 9.60 [b]Rank:[/b] 7 [b]Plays:[/b] 10
[b]2014 Rating:[/b] 9.45 [b]Rank:[/b] - [b]Plays:[/b] 12

RATING: 9
Juegaco de civilizaciones.

Te recordará un poco al TTA ya que la mecánica principal es seleccionar cartas de un track pagando monedas, uno de los tres recursos del juego. 

En esas cartas encontraremos líderes, solo podemos tener uno activo, edificios que generarán recursos si desplegamos en ellos kekos, unidades militares en las que nuevamente desplegaremos kekos, maravillas que construiremos, colonias que conquistaremos y alguna cartica más.

La fuerza militar será importante pero no tan agresiva como en otros juegos de civilizaciones. Marcará el orden de turno y nos permitirá hacer frente a las guerras.

Además hay alguna cosica más, como un track de estabilidad, que te permitirá reducir determinado efectos negativos, al principio del turno tomarás la decisión de crecer (más kekos) o tomar recursos, al final del turno hay un evento etc ...

Si se conoce el juego se juega en un plis y deja unas sensaciones muy buenas.

Nota: he jugado con la expansión Dinastías y me ha gustado. Amén de las cartas nuevas, el concepto de las cartas de dinastía y las maravillas naturales me parece que amplían el juego sin romper nada de su base. Un acierto

RATING: 7.5
First off I have not played TTA so can't compare to that, other than to say I doubt highly this is as long as that full game or even close, 3 newbies played this in 3 hours and we weren't rushing which I understand is not really possible with TTA (for at least 10 or more games, even then I think full game 3 people is more like 4-6). Also this game was VERY easy to learn overall, and I understand TTA is not

Its a classic engine game with a Civ theme, I don't find the theme very strong at all, nothing compared to Pax Porfiriana for sure which has a great strong theme. Speaking of which, both games have a large market of cards to buy from as the central mechanic, along with building your tableau. One of the big differences (there are many small ones) is in the interaction between these two. Here it is in the market competition, and then there is an abstracted war effect maybe once a round where you can be affected, where as in Pax attacking other peoples cards directly is one of the key factors so that is much more interactive (even too much for some surely). 

So the best part of this game imo is the strategic nature, kind of like a deck builder you have great flexibility in how you will build your tableau/Civ, and multiple approaches/paths should work I'm sure and there are a good few ways to score. Basically all the game is here is deciding which cards to buy and when to fit your overall strategy for the game. Now IIRC I think there is a pretty large deck of cards feeding the market, so I don't think you will know just which cards are coming down the pike so you can't plan everything just so. 

The worst part of the game for me was the dumb random event that happened every turn, it just has no place in an otherwise lower luck strategic game. The variable famine amount is not so bad, it is when one player gets arbitrarily shafted that it gets stupid. Its not game breaking, if you are somewhat balanced it shouldn't hamper you too much but it sure doesn't sit well with me.

Anyway its an expensive game for what it is imho, and maybe still a little long so not something I need to rush out and buy but I enjoyed it and would happily play. I'm sure TTA has its pros & cons comparatively, I'm thinking the pros of Nations are worth the Cons it has in comparison but I need to play TTA to know for sure

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This game belongs in the top ten.

Tight design, right play time for the amount of depth, theme "feels" right, once you learn the iconography and turn structure, everything flows well.  Constant, meaningful decisions without induced AP.  These are hallmarks of the "8"s in my collection.

But wait - there's more: in game ability scaling that works quite well with minimal impact, three times as many cards as you would need for a four-player game, in varying configurations of difficulty for all players.  So not only can you handicap yourself relative to other players, you can collectively decide whether you want a tighter, Agricola-esque experience, or a lighter, more Belfort-y experience, where there's light screwage, but everyone basically has the resources to do as they wish.  And the "B" sides of each player board give you the option to remove the structural symmetry and introduce another layer of strategy, if you wish.

Yes, two-player is tense and brutal.  So is Twilight Struggle.  Yes, you can fall behind in the first 30 minutes and not have a great catchup route.  That's true in most systems without luck (be it dice or frequent card draws).

Play this.

RATING: 4.5
I love the concept and idea behind this game but it absolutely fails as a competitive two player game.  I've heard it is good with 4-5 and I don't doubt that at all, but the asymmetrical nations (B Sides) are not balanced with two players.  Less available culture based victory points = some nations have a major edge in end game scoring.  First player is too important and often whomever picks cards first gets the no brainer pick which just makes the rich richer and the other player falls into a death spiral.  The game states that it is designed for 1-5 players so my rating of 2 based on the two player game is justified.  

As a single player game, however, it is not half bad.  I'd give the game a rating of 7 for this player count.

RATING: 5
2014-05-27

Initial Rating: 6.0 (May 2014)

Many friends prefer Nations over Through the Ages (currently my 2nd favorite game of all time), so I was keen on trying Nations but it was hard to getting it to the table. Last Saturday I was able to play it and I found the game to be a letdown, perhaps because I was excepting a GREAT game. Nations is HUGELY TtA derivative, I knew that both games were somewhat similar but they are VERY similar. I can’t help but comparing both games.

I don’t mind long games, what I really hate is the downtime and Nations has downtime in spades. I think it has to be played with 3 players, with more I begin to get bored. I also dislike that I have to begin again when I replace a building or army outfit. This doesn’t have any thematic sense. In Nations it is very hard to get the one resource you are missing because you only have a limited space for buildings on your board. This doesn’t have any thematic sense either. Creating new people in Nations is hugely expensive, their maintenance is always higher that you can repay (in the early stages that gives you a +2 at most of resources) so you are always going to be in the negative. This doesn’t have any sense to me and I don’t like it. A hard game is generally a positive thing for me and I will have to play again to see if this is the case with Nations or not.

Military power is very important, many friends said that military wasn’t as important as it is on TtA but it is. I know maintaining a strong military force takes a lot of resources, but the negatives of losing a war can be devastating. In TtA when you lose a war you can end losing up the game right there so wars are a lot more forgiving in Nations, but still very important. The bad news is that the turn order is also decided by the military power allowing for runaway leaders and the weaker get weaker situations. The strong always get first chance to get the better cards. 

Oversized box, I was expecting better components due to its price and box size, the art and components are merely ok. 

For now I will play Nations again to see if I like it better. On its own it is a good game but it doesn´t come close to TtA greatness.

Current Rating: 6.0

RATING: 9
I had my fair share of doubts when coming into trying this game out as I was already had my standards set so high by being avid fan of Sid Mieier's Civilization video games.

Of course, it wouldn't be much fair to compare video game to board game, but with this specific Civ building games, much focus and feel of the game is the accomplishment of how your civilization came about, and flourished or perished all while having tied into historical backgrounds. 

Does Nations satisfied me? Yes, it did but I still need to play a bit more. Not a complex game, but the strategy is heavy, and few more plays will sort out unease feelings that I have about this game.

+ Nice little touch on back of cards that progressively gain colors as ages pass. Little things like this makes me happy :).

+ Simple rules. The game is so simple to teach yet the strategy behind this game is immerse. 

+ The strategy in this game is huge. More I play this game, I discover so much more paths to victory. My first few were like ok...I really don't know what I am doing. But after seen some cards, how the interactions better work out, and grasp the flow of the game better, it opened up totally new stage. It was like Mage Knight experience all over again except Nations is much easier game.

+/- It is not very strategical, but rather you will have to deal with what is best given to you at the moment. You cannot go into this game thinking that I will build a massive army and stomp over others - rather you will need to be flexible to be able to adept to any changes. Much akin to RftG in that regard. This can be pro and con, but for me I don't mind it too much as I like tactical games. This does not mean that overall strategy is not absolutely viable - just prepare to change. 

+ Perhaps it is that likeness to RftG which draws me so much into this game. For solo, it plays fairly quickly (~30 mins), has Civ building theme (although you don't really feel acomplished looking at 4 buildings and few wonders in the end), and packed with meaningful choices on each round as what is the best course of action to take.

- The components feel very cheap. The box is unnecessarily big but I am not sure whether this was to accommodate for more expansions coming. Cardboard bits are just barely serviceable. If the components were any less in quality, I would have cried foul. I have Caverna and MK besides Nations which I paid even less than Nations: needless to say, I was unimpressed. As a paying customer, I demand more for my hard earned money.

- Only Industrial age? I know that they cut it down for playing time issues but I wanted to see modern day achievements and scientific development cards. RATING: 8
Moving card display, resource acquisition and payment, population usage and management, buildings, wonders, colonies, all of these facets can equally apply to Nations and Through the Ages. I would venture to say Nations wouldn't exist without Vlaada's ground-breaking work on TtA. While it is clearly derivative, Nations offers a completely different play experience than TtA.

Nations simplifies the game by reducing the dependencies and interactions between cards and removing or streamlining all of the penalties for high production and population growth. Also, there is no concept of "government" in Nations and government might be THE defining feature of any empire. As a result, Nations lacks the feeling of political struggle and incremental growth that TtA captures really well. In Nations, much of this boils down to "what do I need more or less of next turn? let me adjust". That's not the say the game isn't fun or interesting, it's just different. Nations is about acquiring cards that you need and then populating and using them to manage your growth effectively. Nations feels more like an optimization exercise of upgrading cards and shifting resources. In what it does do, it is one of the better mid-weight euros I've played this year. 

Happy to own this and enjoy it when I want a mid-weight euro. While similar in many ways to TtA, they aren't even trying to do the same thing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I think I went through the 5 stages of loss and grief when playing this game multiple times. At first it was denial as I decided that I didn't want to invest in military and thought that it would be a good idea. When that plan didn't work I was then naturally quite angry. I tried to bargain with the game by saying that it was just the way the cards came out. Depression soon followed as I figured out it was not the game I hoped it would be. Luckily acceptance was next because it is a nice game in it's own right. 

Seriously though, I like this game quite a bit. The first play left me cold and bitter as I thought that there was no viable strategy without military in this game. I mean like 2/3 of the cards are dedicated to military. The second time I tried it, I won using no military whatsoever. So I was glad to see that the game was balanced enough to include pacifists like myself. 

Still, I can see the complaints regarding the dynamic nature of the card draws. There are so many cards in each deck that you can quite easily have a miserable game since the cards just come out wrong. 

One of my buddies mentioned the similarities to 7 Wonders and I can definitely see what he's saying. I think I need to give this one another play or two just to see how much I like it. 

It is definitely worth a play though!

de 1 a 5, tablero

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Nations promises to be what I wished Through the Ages was. Micro-managing your empire in TTA was highly enjoyable on your turn. It dragged on other players' turns. It was killed for me when the wars in the third age came up and it became obvious that there's no alternative strategy to developing military might.
Nations seems to solve these issues. It's streamlined, each player only takes small turns avoiding ten-minute breaks between your turns and the fiddliness of TTA is gone. It's possible to pursue non-military strategies by either declaring a minor war yourself to preempt players with strong military from waging war or by upgrading the stability of your civilization to weather any upcoming war.
As a whole, TTA is better designed and deeper but Nations is more for me although there are more rough edges. It's too early to tell if there are other more severe weaknesses, dominant strategies or strategic musts. Until then, I remain optimistic on Nations.

RATING: 5
It is very clearly TtA with the serial numbers filed off, and much of the streamlining done has some merit (e.g. teaching Nations probably takes 20% of the time of doing it for TtA). But fundamentally what was gained in shorter playtime was lost in compelling gameplay.

Never ever play with all the cards mixed together. The card decks are way too large compared to the number of cards seen, which makes streaky and abnormal card draws way too likely. The game is somewhat better with just the basic deck or the competitive game variant, since the card distribution will be more sensible. However, this still won't help too much with the event card distribution. And no matter what, the gameplay is very tactical. 

A special demerit for making the base game box contain huge amounts of components that apparently nobody should play with. Usually you'd at least wait for an expansion before destroying a game like that.

RATING: 10
A relatively quickly playing civ game with enough interaction and no direct conflict. That's definitely a game for me. There are also some nice touches that remind of Sid Meier's computer games which splendidly add to the atmosphere. Overall, this is about drafting the best civ stuff from a public board and really tight resource management that eases up a bit towards the end as your engine rolls smoother. I really like the handling of military and wars and the balance within those as it's perfectly possible to run a successful pacifist strategy. And that's really a must have option for a civ game, because otherwise it's a war game. The stability is also a super neat mechanism. Different plays of Nations offer a huge amount of variability and there's a lot, lot, lot of replayability.

RATING: 6
This civilization game aims to take most of the elements from Through the Ages (notably excluding government) and put them in a similar but gentler and more manageable mould. Some parts feel smoother, there are fewer miniature bits, it scales more handily, warfare is not explicitly targeted, strategic commitment is limited, thematic matching is all over the place. There's definitely more randomness, in the events that may or may not reward specialization or punish neglect as well as in the draft with its relative lack of look-ahead. Not sure about the balance, but then I don't know about TtA either. It is also a long game, though not quite as long as TtA at any given player count.

RATING: 7
First impression.  I enjoyed this game.  It ran a little long, so I would hope it can speed up a tick.  The theme is good.  I sensed a strong bias towards heavy military development.  So while this looks like a Civ game, it felt more like a conflict game.  

But the conflict is not map based.  You merely damage the other players resources/scores.  The game system felt tough.  I mainly struggled against the system, the adversity from the other players as less than half of the struggle.

Initially, I was dubious that I wanted extra workers - the price seemed quite high.  But having them out there and working is a boon for endgame scoring.  Better to have them, and break even maintaining them, as they will pay off at the end.

If this boils down to a 2 hour game, I might just have to buy a copy for game nights at the club.  If it stays at the 3 hour mark, I will likely wait for it to arrive once in a while.

RATING: N/A
From the humble beginnings of civilization through the historical ages of progress, mankind has lived, fought, and built together in nations. Great nations protect and provide for their own, while fighting and competing against both other nations and nature itself. Nations must provide food and stability as the population increases. They must build a productive economy. And all the while, they must amaze the world with their great achievements to build up their heritage as the greatest nations in the history of mankind!

Nations is an intense historical board game for 1–5 players that takes 40 minutes per player to play. Players control the fate of nations from their humble start in prehistoric times until the beginning of World War I. The nations constantly compete against each other and must balance immediate needs, long-term growth, threats, and opportunities.

RATING: 6.2
I still have not played Through the Ages so I can not make comparisons here. Nations is a civilisation game with lots of cards, some worker placementish mechanism (but your workers are not competing for common action spaces), a few distinct resource types, a few different card types (for some reason, feeling like - probably only because of the artwork - somewhat Sankt Petersburg-like)... and nothing really outstanding. This is not a bad game but I can't name anything to recommend it for. For such a long civilisation game I didn't even really feel the theme which should be the point of it all. Heck, I didn't even feel a real story arc to the game. But yes, this game is rather long (3 hours or more) and when in a long game like this there is no catch-up mechanism it is a bit painful to have a clear winner after one hour or so. I did not hate the experience at all but can't name a civilisation game (long or short) I enjoyed less than this one either. I mean, hey, why is it rated and ranked higher than Sid Meier's Civilization: The Board Game, The Setlers of Catan, La Cittá, Roll Through the Ages: The Bronze Age, Rise of Empires, Olympos, Olympus and so on (it is even rated higher than Tigris & Euphrates and 7 wonders)? I guess that must be some cult of the new hype.

edit, one year later: yes it seems it is - now Tigris and 7 wonders are above this one at least.

RATING: 4
It's a civilization building game similar to Thought the ages which has no world map.

It's basically a resource management game where it's hard to be balanced in all type of resources. There is a worker placement aspect to the game,  but I don't find it problematic because people place workers on their own board. So there is no scaling problems with various amount of players. Also the workers stay there from turn to turn but they can be moved by paying the cost.

If you like resource management games, then this game should be for you, but there are a few issues I want to point out.

First, you can get extra workers but their maintenance cost is so expensive that it's impossible to use that extra worker to even repay it's maintenance cost. So I think the wheat maintenance cost are way too strong. -1 or -2 wheat would have been more acceptable than -3.

While those that gives -3 stability, that's a huge  drawback considering My stability has been wondering between 2-4 for the whole game. Until I realized that there are no penalty for being in revolt beside being last all the time. So I could just take all 4 meaples with -12 stability, be last all the time, and it would have been better than taking no meaple and be last half of the time.

Battle cards are also not more effective as the ages progress. All cards becomes more and more powerful as the age progress except the battle cards. I know that they are variable to your unit strength, but still, a bonus, or a different selection of resource could have been interesting.

Finally, I don't know why publishers thinks you have unlimited space to store your game because the box of the game is so huge for what it has. 

Second, the game is not worth 100$. Seriously, there are a bunch of meeples, 1 board, a few sheets, and cards. It would have fit in a thinner box or in half the box if the board was foldable. 

Class of cultures has cool miniatures and it's selling price is lower (80$).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This game deserves an 11
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A good civilization game, somewhat similar with "Through The Ages", but with much more variability (card decks). It has a great theme with hsistoric elements just like Through The Ages.  War/aggression and events tactics overall have a little less of an impact but randomness could create major shifts on your Nation but you can usually see bad things coming that round, unlike in TtA.  In TtA, you can be unknowingly blindsided by (unseen) Event cards, but in Nations, you can account for and plan for mitigating circumstances.  The only issue with Nations is that if one player does poorly, it will unravel unto other players significantly more or conversely help other Nations improve.  

My biggest thing against Nations (being a "great" game) is that I wish the card display were one additional column or one additional row more for more cards.  If certain cards (resource producers) do not come out sufficient enough, this can create havoc with one or two nations, which mostly unravels and creates a falling behind problem.  I would suggest an addition column of cards based on # of players (maybe three or four more).  Just a little, not too much more.

[In terms of comparing such civilization/nation building games, I also really like Clash of Cultures.  CoC does not have the historical theme elements but the game play and mechanics are a little better when you play with the variable ending, which I personally prefer.]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
First Impression:

I can understand the comparisons to Through the Ages.  This one is lighter and plays much more simply.  Calculating military and keeping track of production and population is much easier to do in this one.  There is still the same tension between trying to build a balanced (or not) engine to produce what you need in the moment.  This game feels a bit more tactical in nature as you respond to the event and the game in the current round of the age more so than what you know may be coming down the road.  Just because this game distills Civilization down to the basic essence does not mean one cannot be destroyed - quite easy actually for this game to knock you down and keep one down but there is often also room to get back and get back into the fight.  

The massive amount of cards in this game means that no two games will ever be the same.  One can never count on cards to come out on the display.  While military is more indirect and effects everyone (instead of TtA's targeted attacks), there can still be a considerable pain factor.  There are ways to mitigate the damage if one increases one's stability factor high enough - might only lose one VP.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
I like this game more than [b]Through the Ages[/b]. The wars and events can really cripple you, but nothing compared to Napoleon's Holy Air Strikes . Even though you can not attack a certain player, there is still a lot of player interactions. You have to be on your toes all the time and to be sure no one can cause you to lose your position if they do everything they can to do it. If you pass too early, people will built their armies and start a war which you are going to lose. Or they increase their Stability and cause you to lose something precious (Workers, Food, Culture or even Victory Points). Losing Wars isn't always bad as you can negate the effects with high Stability, but you will always lose 1 Victory Point.

The game is more tactical than strategical and that's probably why I like it better than [b]TtA[/b], in which I usually end up short of everything and get beaten up badly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I'm ought to warn people that that one can end up in a negative spiral, which significantly lowers the enjoyment.

RATING: 7
Another go at the three hour Civ game, and one that might be reaching closer to the grail than most attempts in the past. A lot of things work very well, from the tableau of available cards to the resource differentation, but my favorite is probably how the workers are handled. Most things that did not work quite as well can be blamed more on the haphazard rule book than on the game itself.

The current rating of 7 is rather lowballing it; my first and only game so far felt more like an 8. I am, however, a bit afraid of how the game will be balanced out once the we progress to the advanced card decks and the culture tableaus. Streakiness of the deck might become an issue, I guess. If those fears turn out to be unfounded, this will be a keeper.

RATING: 8
My favorite civ builder. It plays fast and stays true to civilization building. Each era brings fun upgrades and no one strategy will dominate every game. Setup time can be on the long side, but a good organizer drops it significantly.

RATING: 8
My first Civ game besides 7 Wonders, so i can't compare it to others in the genre really. First impressions, it took forever to play. i like euros, but 3 hours later, i was wore out. I enjoyed how your workers stayed on the board as long as you wanted them there. I also enjoyed the importance of military, and how you need it for raiding as well as winning wars. I liked how you were penalized for not winning the war, or for being last place in some of the round's objectives. I thought that penalizing players added a great tension to the game, although it wasn't forgiving to the players who were in last place; that kind of turned it into a the rich get richer and the poor get poorer kinda situation. The rounds just took forever...i wish you could do most of your actions simultaneously rather than taking turns. and the iconography on the cards can be a little confusing, i found myself having to explain which one pays now and which one pays later a few times, as well as explaining that the stability track always adjusts cause its not just a one time thing. But overall i enjoyed the experience of playing Nations, i found it unique and challenging.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
What I like:
- Tight resources
- Somewhat forgiving if you don't have enough resources
- Not much downtime between turns
- Losses from wars/events are fixed
- The cost isn't too high to get into later age technologies
- No limit on the number of actions you can do per turn as long as you have the resources

What I didn't like:
- Gameplay is still too long for us.  A 4-player game takes us 2.5-3 hours
- The event/war penalties in the later ages are pretty painful
- There isn't really a sense of upgrading because you have to repay to place your workers when you cover an existing building
- Your civilization can change quite drastically from turn to turn since each turn you're focusing on the current event.

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Received in trade for Sid Meier's Civilization with Wisdom and Warfare 7/15/16.

Low downtime due to quick turn mechanics.  The culture track is less dynamic at 2p, but the game does become VERY cuthroat.  Replayability is through the roof.  Adding in the Dynasties expansion yields 17 very different nations to play with.  Each game also sees different cards, as we only go through maybe 1/5 of each age's deck per game.  For a relatively abstracted card game, a lot of it feels thematic.  Components are pretty bad quality though, especially considering MSRP.

We really enjoy how the battles and military aren't very "Take-That" and aren't overly mean.  The occasional war can sometimes really screw someone over, but it's not malicious like it is in Sid Meier's Civ.

When combining decks, it's completely necessary to institute the lower variance variant.

RATING: N/A
Purchased from: Geek Bay (BGG)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I really like tactical, chaotic games such as Nations. If you don't, or if you're looking for more of a feeling of genuinely building a civilization, stick with TtA.

In Nations, each round is its own mini-puzzle with your incentives based on that round's randomly drawn event as well as a looming threat of war. Speaking of which, war seems to be a huge part of the game. If both your military and civil happiness track are low, you will be constantly screwed by the war cards and left spinning your wheels, unable to grow your civilization.

The handicapping method (King, Chieftain, etc.) is novel and allows people at different skill levels to compete.

The endgame is somewhat unsatisfying with simply adding up points on cards and 1/10 VP each for all other resources and tracks.

For me, the game runs a shade long (2.5 hr for 3 players) to be pulled out during every session.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5

I don't think this game will ever reach my Top 5 Games though. It's pretty long and even though the pacing is fine, there isn't enough immersion and there isn't anything truly clever or elegant about the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
03/15/14 Played a three-player game in 3.5 hours, at Chieftain level, using the "A" sides of the player mats. 
I own Through the Ages, and I was quite taken aback how this game basically copies about 80% of that game. Yet for some reason Nations costs almost twice as much even though it has less stuff in the box! I like TtA so I was predisposed to like Nations. The rules says a three-player game should take two hours. Ha! It took us almost twice that long. Maybe, just maybe, experienced players could get 'r done in two hours, but I doubt it. I do not think I'd want to play the game with a full compliment of five; it would take too long. I can't help but compare Nations to Through the Ages. Nations is a little shorter, a little less complex, and a little less fiddly. Yet that comes at a price, as one receives less satisfaction in shaping/tailoring one's empire. I do find it odd that advisors can remain alive and active the entire game (while leaders and scientists in TtA die off regularly, as they should). The war mechanism in Nations is OK, but it really doesn't feel like you're fighting (its more like a plague that players can inoculate themselves against), whereas wars in TtA do. Nations is a good game, but Through the Ages is a little better. 
08/16/14 Played a four-player game in four hours, at Chieftain level, using the "A" sides of the player mats. 
07/11/15 Played a three-player game in three hours, at Prince level, using the "B" sides of the player mats.

RATING: 8
Nearly impossible not to mention [thing=25613]that other civ game[/thing]. You draft cards: leaders, wonders and technology are still here. You can build up your army but now it's not the same arms race and you can take a defensive stance instead. What's really makes Nations stand out is that players aren't taking all of their actions at once. Instead you perform a micro-action and that continues around the table until you pass out of the round. Nations is more a game of timing. The decisions are still tough but it's not the AP-fest that pushed TtA into the computer-only category for me. 

UPDATE: Knocked down a point, already in my second game. Came across a scenario where I just continued to lose points and get further behind making for a long, frustrating night.

RATING: 8
One cannot play or review Nations without comparing it to Through the Ages. TtA stands out as the superior game from a design standpoint; it's balanced, beautiful, brutal. But I believe Nations might be more fun. First and foremost, it is less punishing, and this allows play between players of a greater skill disparity (even without the overt handicap capability). This also makes it less depressing. While I can't say there are more paths to victory, as TtA contains dozens of combos and such, Nations feels more open to more styles of play, and that makes it less stressful and more enjoyable for me. I need to give it more plays, but so far I like it.

RATING: 9
We've thoroughly enjoyed our first ten games. It's not particularly difficult to learn, but your appreciation of the game grows with experience. I've never played TTA as it seemed too long, but I always regretted that I missed out. No longer. We play two player games of Nations in 90 minutes. We are totally absorbed. It's tense, competitive, and very challenging. Compliments to the designers for the game's flexibility, variability, and depth, and to their willingness to answer rules questions. If you're an experienced games it will take you several games until you're ready for the full suite of cards and the B sides. And you should have no trouble learning the game in its two player version. This is one of the most interesting and elegant games I've played this year.

There's a bunch of cards of several different types, a bunch of them come out every round, and you have to react and figure out which ones to go after. Rinse and repeat. I'm horrible at Saint Petersburg, and seemingly equally horrible at Nations. I tend to be a slow, plodding thinker, and as such I don't do well at games that require me to quickly assess a situation and choose the right tactical response. It's just too much for me to absorb, and so I'm stuck between either making a quick choice which is almost always wrong, or getting all AP and taking horribly long turns. Neither choice is very appealing, but I hate being the AP guy at the table, so I usually just choose the first option, with predictable results. 

Add in the fact that your opponents can do things that royally screw you over (although admittedly, it's not nearly as bad in Nations as it is in Through the Ages), and the result is that I just end up making a giant mess of things. And if you're like me and not fully grasping everything that's going on at any given moment, then those screw-you moves always seem to come out of left field, at the worst possible moment.

I suppose if I played it a few more times and gained some familiarity with the cards and the different civilizations, I might be able to play semi-competently, but I'm not sure the payoff is worth it. I seriously doubt I'll ever win at this game, no matter how many times I play.

RATING: 8
Watered down TtA with massively streamlined (too streamlined maybe?) mechanics, plays quickly, no fiddly runaway circular chits, players now take turns to take actions which makes loads more sense, better art than TtA though the art is not actually that great, neat solo rules, interesting 'solution' to wars which now affect everyone, variable civ powers, no modern era (did they hold this back for an expansion?) which I miss, tidier concept of nation Stability, all around pretty great Diet implementation of Vlaada's masterpiece.

Oddly enough this will probably see a lot more playtime than TtA because of its streamlined nature.


UPDATE 07/02/14:

This was an intriguing prospect from the outset, a pared down TtA without all the hassle of setup and tear-down, taking it in turns to take turns - like Eclipse (instead of taking all your actions at once in TtA and then going to put the pizzas in whilst you wait for your next turn), less hassle of upkeep and corruption, more even-handed wars that don’t effectively remove players from the game for hours, etc. So I squeezed it in as a last minute Christmas 2013 purchase before the gaming embargo slammed down. It had good stock for me coming from the Lautapeli guys who made Eclipse, and when I opened the box I was only mildly disappointed to see that they’d taken the bland graphic design of TtA and made it…. slightly less bland. The artwork is a moderate improvement, and the variety of cards is great. That you’ll only get to see a handful of them each game increases replayability but changes it up from a strategic race to grab the cards you know are eventually coming (in TtA) to a reactive, tactical game based on what might be coming down the line. It’s unlikely but you might never see any Leaders for example. Or Advisors as Nations calls them.
It plays a hell of a lot faster than TtA - in one session in January we were able to play three games in one evening. The solo option is great too and as I’ve said elsewhere it is a doddle to grasp, but a bugger to master, as it should be, and is a great tool for learning the game. There are extra event tiles included specifically for the solo game and a lot of thought has clearly gone into this side of it, which I appreciate, and it rates your play by comparing you to a famous leader like the old Civ PC games used to, so you have a score to strive to beat each time.
I've played about 8 solo games now and would happily break it out again soon. This potentially gives me an unfair advantage in multiplayer games, but you can (and I have) offset that by using the included setup rules to start off on a harder level to other players by producing less resources each turn.
On the whole, I love it and think it will replace TtA in 'real life' for the most part, mainly due to play time, though I will never part with my TtA.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is exceptionally good. It's like Through the Ages in that you use purchase and use cards to build up your empire. It's quicker, slightly easier, and most importantly less fiddly than TTA in terms of pushing and managing cubes. 

The game is more random than TTA because not all cards will come out everygame, which makes it an even more tactical game than TTA - some may find this an advantage, some may not.

I feel that the freshness of the game, and the slightly more variable games and the fact that it is easier and shorter, makes me want to play this over TTA right now. 

However, one minor issue of the game is it can be frustrating to play if you are being put into a poor position (either because you played poorly or you just have bad luck) Unlike TTA, such consqeuence can be justified mostly because of your poor moves.

RATING: 7.5
In this Civilization building game, each player first chooses a nation (Persia, Egypt, China, Greece, Rome). Over 8 rounds, players take actions: choosing a card from the display, moving an architect to a wonder, deploying a worker, or using special actions on cards. Each round there is an event that one has to manage, and there could be wars. Wars are resolved by comparing the military value of the player who started the war to others to see their affect on you. Stability can be used to mitigate wars, while books help you gain VPs.  At the beginning of each round, each player also chooses resources from food/stone/ore/worker. 

This is a very engaging game, as you slowly build up your civilization. You are always on the knife edge, as one mistake could collapse your game. There is a ton of tension as you try to manage your nation. It's a decent game, but it can be long (my first game clocked in at 5 hours for four people), but I was fully engaged the whole time. I also do worry about someone making a mistake and being out of it early.  Still, I'm looking forward to more plays. I think the sweet spot for this game is with 3 players.

RATING: 8
Simple and brief rules, and the gameflow is very elegant; the game solves all the issues with fast and simple mechanics.

These simple rules are decitful: they create a very deep game.

The mechanic of doing one action at a time (instead of the full turn, like TTA) creates tension and dynamism.

Events, cards and the action of the other players will create very different games, and preset strategies will never be used. Replayability will not be an issue.

The players have different abilities and starting resources that feels different (not a lot, but it's not "only 1 special feature per race"), but contrary to other games, this doesn't mark their strategy to be followed on the long run.

Things change a lot during the 8 turns that make each game. You can be the most militaristic nation one turn, and 2 turns after have no army at all. This provides a sense of history and civilization that very few games do.

Another difference with the average games of this genre: miltary is still a key aspect, but it's not possible for several players to join their forces to demolish another one.

Idiom is not a big barrier.


On the other side, games are longer than advertised. My first game (5 players) lasted 4 hours. I think it should have been designed as a 4 player maximum.

Contrary to what I've read, it's not possible to win without building a heavy military (at least once in the game).

I might increase the rating after more games.

BTW, I didn't mention this game is heavily (very heavily) based on Through The Ages. It's a streamlined TTA.

RATING: 7
OK. Let's do a little comparison exercise, shall we?

Nations was released at Essen 2013. I didn't buy it at first, although it was on my wishlist. And, without any doubt even now, justifiably so. When I played it a few months later, it was an enjoyable streamlined slider-balancing for 2-5 players.

One game I DID receive from my Essen Wishlist was Prosperity. It was an enjoyable, streamlined slider-balancing for 2-4 players.

Notice anything familiar here? Ultimately, I rank this one lower due to its higher cost, longer play time and poorly-integrated chrome.

Nations is more or less EXACTLY THE SAME GAME as Prosperity. It has added a more glorious theme, and a token fifth player, sure. But it has also taken away: in the random and ultimately game-changing event cards

Why is one ranked nearly 2000 places below the other on this site? God only knows...

RATING: 9
comment after 3 games: this game IS a reduced version of Through The Ages, there are a lot of identical aspects. Its most important difference is the (slightly) better components and that it can be explained to a newbie withing 15 minutes and without any preliminary training session!
Some aspects of TTA have been simplified, in particular the production of science, and this is bad. Moreover, battles are ridiculously identical: it doesn't matter whether you are at Cannes or at Waterloo, the result is the same: choice among rocks, food and culture.
However the game flows smoothly, it can be played immediately by new players, there is a very large variety of situations, mostly due to the fact that less than half the cards are used per game.
Very very good work! I would only add some flavor to battle and wars artworks.

RATING: 7

Thus, the game's fairly engaging theme and gameplay are marred by poor balance and randomness. The victor is determined not by strategy, but by the luck of the cards that turn up in the final rounds.

RATING: 8
A nice streamlining of Through The Ages. While TtA was about the cards but also had a ton of accounting, this is all about the cards. You start with 5 cards, each of which give you various combinations of money, stone, culture, military, and food. Allocate your 5 meeples among these as you please, and that sets out what your economy will produce. Use gold to buy more efficient cards to replace those you have. Reallocate your meeples to the more efficient cards using stone. Make sure you get enough allocated to military, because even though there's no direct aggression, being first in military gives you first pick of the new cards next turn (awesome advantage) as well as avoid penalties whenever someone pays for a war card. You can also buy cards to give you one-off benefits, cards that you can pay stone to build wonders (for ongoing benefits), and country cards you can take if you have high enough military (for more ongoing benefits). All of which should sound eerily similar to TtA fans. Build up your culture production as most culture each round earns VPs, but there's plenty of VPs in building higher level stuff as well. Once you've spent your gold, racing in turn order to draft the best cards, then it's just a matter of doing the non-player-interactive stuff, like allocating your meeples. So reviewing the new cards and re-evaluating when your pets are taken is where the downtime is. The decision tradeoffs are the inevitable ones of foregoing military and/or culture progression for a better production engine, short term vs long term gains and the like. Other than that, the game's straightforward and relatively easy to teach. There's an event to compete for each round, produce stuff based on how you've set your meeples up, pay for more meeples, etc. Standard stuff, but done neatly. There's oodles of cards, and the order in which they come out makes for different strategies and should make for variety from game to game. As long as you play with fast-ish players, and maybe 3 players max, it should feel about the right length (but it has the possibility to drag out otherwise). There's obviously plenty to explore, which is always attractive.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

RATING: 5
A brilliant piece of game design that seems to do 95% of what Through the Ages does in a fraction of the time. However, I am rating it a seven because even though it is a brilliant game design, I find it too math-heavy for my taste and it simply takes too long to complete with five players. There are a lot of moving parts and the design is largely successful. However, there are to "gamey" aspects to it. For example, we are building a civilization and yet, hoarding resources and selling them for victory points has nothing to do with the theme and seems to be a shortcut for the designers to better balance the game. 

I would probably rate it an 8 or even a 9 with four or less players (three players may be the sweet spot for this game). With five, it overstays its welcome by a couple of hours.

Update: I lowered it two points because the new version of Through the Ages is deeper and more fun, I believe. In fairness, there is nothing wrong with this game and the time commitment is more reasonable than TTA. It is just that, for me, TTA provides a more compelling narrative and satisfying gaming experience.

RATING: 7
I really enjoy this game, mainly due to the civ-building theme. The various cards present a lot of interesting decisions as you try to maximize them for your current strategy. It is also neat how you can quickly ramp your military up or down as needed for the current situation. Military is important in this game, but you don't use it to directly attack the other players, which is one aspect of other civ-building games that I do not enjoy.

I think three is the sweet spot for number of players, especially if they already know the game. Four is good also but gets a little long. I don't know that I would like to play five...

Two players is enjoyable but is much more confrontational due to the "most / least" criteria on the event cards. It is not one my wife liked a lot.

The bot tiles are where I get stuck). So that was a big disappointment - I just play 2 player vs myself instead.

Ok so based on this post, designer Rustan H contacted me and directed me to the updated rules on BGG which are somewhat clearer. Still, even with them I had to go to You Tube to find answers to my questions. Now that I have a few proper solo plays under my best,  I can say that this is one of the best solo experiences out there - strategic, different each time, great replay value and you actually feel like you have a purpose.

Later:

Have played this multiplayer now and it is a very strong medium engine builder. Very very good strategy game... however it is possible to have a bad start and be out of the running from quite early on and this is something I see as a bit of a flaw. I know you can avoid using the more advanced cards until you get the hang of the game but where's the fun in that?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Nations is billed as a streamlined Through the Ages, and I think it hits that target pretty well.  At its core it's a card drafting game--the main action is players taking turns buying cards off a central display.  These cards feed into your economy, your military strength, or stability, which allows you to mitigate the negative effects of having a poor military.  There's a way to get extra workers to be able to use your cards more times, but it's really, really expensive.  Managing your economy is a lot simpler than in Through the Ages--the depth the TtA provides in this respect is what makes me think that it's still a better game.  Nations is definitely an enjoyable take on the genre, though.

(2 plays)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm unsure of how to rate this game. It's like a more tactical version of Through the Ages with a lot more luck thrown in. The game plays quite a bit quicker and there is very little downtime. It's like it stripped away all the rough edges of Through the Ages, but left a soulless husk in it's wake.

I enjoy the game, it's no TtA replacement, but it does work better with 4 and 5 players. I also realize how much I enjoy seeding the event deck in TtA compared to a random flip of a card in Nations.

[b]Update:[/b] Alright, after a few more games and even a solo game, I've come away with a better appreciation for the game. No, it's not a TTA killer. It's almost in the same vein as Eldritch is to Arkham, although not quite as direct. I wish there was an easier way to implement the Advanced/Expert cards. Best solution I've seen so far is to just shuffle them all together, discard anything beyond the basic deck card counts (4 of everything, 8 of the blue ones). Not perfect, but it beats trying to break down, shuffle together the extra cards, and reseed the deck. I have to figure out how to adjust the deck for a 5 player game. Maybe 2 more cards of each type and 4 of the blue?

The rating may rise a point just due to how smooth the solo game played. It felt more like a challenge instead of "score the most points".

Includes Grand Duchy or Finland promo.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a card based Civ game, where you have this player board with a limited number of slots for cards. You buy cards from a main board, and adjust your resource incomes accordingly. You also have a stability stat and a military stat, both of which are used to acquire certain cards or extra abilities. I was initially skeptical I would like this game, since I didn't really connect with Through the Ages: A Story of Civilization and I tent to prefer 'dudes on the map' style civ games. This game reminded me a lot of a heavier Legacy: The Testament of Duke de Crecy which I do like a lot. I've come to understand I really enjoy right resource management games with chartsy personal boards. In particular, I liked how while you were in tight competition with other players, the negative repercussions for losing in a war could be mitigated. The way you can place multiple workers on buildings one per turn at a time was also fantastic as it ads a push your luck element to the areas of tight competition. 

:nostar: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::star::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Played my second game of Nations last night and from the first/second turn, it was apparent that there was no way of getting back into the game after making a mistake. Aside from the handicap mechanism, there is nothing dynamic in the game that helps a trailing player (such as in The Voyages of Marco Polo, where the dice roll is bad, you get compensation, or in Power Grid, you get first dibs on resources and cities) and for me, it is a broken game.

RATING: 8
This is a great empire building game.  The rules are well written, easy to understand and teach.  Often these types of games have complex rules that keep you from going back to them as you have to read the rules over again.  I don't see that issue here.  The game had great historical flavor and I really felt like I was building my civilization.  With a lot of cards and the different cards in every game there is a lot of re-playability.  The one tough thing is that it is an unforgiving game.  If you get off to a bad start and do not get the game turned around quickly then you may be in for a long game knowing you cant win.  I am looking forward to playing this game a lot.

RATING: 9
I had modest expectations, having strongly disliked Through the Ages. But this is an excellent civ-building game, with tension, variability, broad strategic horizons.

The five civilizations feel distinct. The card array presents extremely difficult choices, and the opportunity to build distinctive civilizations and pursue a variety of approaches of victory. It's a long game, but with only one action per player at a time, it clicks along nicely.

It may have a runaway leader problem. Or rather, early mistakes can be crippling. But I don't know what a catch-up mechanism would be welcome here. The heart of the gameplay is the tension of keeping your civilization afloat in the early ages, and pursuing a coherent strategy in the later ages. And there is genuine interaction here; if you fail to track you opponents' needs and goals, you'll be at a handicap.

I've played with 3, 4, and 5, and each works fine, though five will obviously run longer. The variety of cards offers tremendous replayability. Oh, and the production values are fine - I have no idea what people complaining about.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Even at the basic level (A Boards, Basic Cards), players will spend the first game studying the cards not knowing what might be coming up.  This can leave players feeling less in control of the game and more subject to the vagaries of the card tableau.  I expect (much like 7 Wonders, Peloponnes, and other nation building games) that familiarity will bring with it a better appreciation of the game flow and how to manage it.

I do like the individual player adjustment level as a method of balancing the game between experienced and beginning players, but the experienced player will have the card knowledge advantage.

The 40 minutes / player is a good estimate and the game is rather simplistic as far as game mechanisms go (buy a card or place a worker or place an architect), so the challenge is managing the resources and the potential against future cards/events.

Less intermeshed systems than Through the Ages, but might be the game to get for the quicker experience of such a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Has improved with further play. Over a dozen games now and not getting tired of it. I do prefer Civ Through the Ages over this, but this is a friendlier version of that and more requested by others. I'm not sold on the final age and the often pointless leaders, events, etc.

The Finnish version of Civilization: Through The Ages. Certainly it's a less mean game than Civilization: Through The Ages. Military takes more of a back seat, although it's still very much important (it determines turn order after all), it's less crippling to those on the wrong side of the sword point. Who's the target audience of this game? It seems to be reaching out for the same gaming audience that plays Civilization: Through The Ages. So were they specifically targeting gamers who found Civilization: Through The Ages a bit not to their liking for one reason or another, and those that like Civilization: Through The Ages so much that they'd be interested in playing a refined version of said game? (Either way, I think they've succeeded.) The two games are so mind glowingly similar and yet so different. From the colours of the cards, wonder steps and costs, the selection mechanic and multiple other similarities. And yet, this is a refined version of Civilization: Through The Ages and there are so many tweaks that the games feel the same, but also don't feel the same. It's weird. I can't judge how easy everything being familiar made it to learn, but it was extremely easy to pick up and launch into.

Although they've made it less fiddly, there's still an awful lot of counting. There's the familiar military level, maintaining workers (but not with happiness), and production counting. These are all things that become second nature to an experienced Civilization: Through The Ages player and so it's the same with Nations.

The number one improvement Nations has over Civilization: Through The Ages is keeping the pace up by having each player perform one action then onto the next player, instead of all their actions in a go. They've limited the number of colonies, wonders, resources type you can have. They've made it less war like by limiting it to one war that affects everyone (or possibly no one), building a way to lessen the impact of war and overall making it less crushing while still keeping the importance of the military level for turn order, events and conflicts multipliers. There's so many cards that aren't going to be seen by players from game to game that it'll definitely be less open to expert player planning than Civilization: Through The Ages. In this respect the game is better with more players because you see more cards from the decks. There's no secret cards, but that's mitigated by the uncertainty of what cards will appear from game to game. So there's no scoring cards you've discarded that you're opponents don't know about. All this makes the game a very different beast. Perhaps less tense. To an extent it feels like you're playing more against the game than against the other players, especially when you play at the highest difficulty level - all the more so than when compared against that Czech game. After six games I've come tot he realisation that players probably have a better time at the easier levels because they can do more stuff. 

In it's own right it's a great game. A lot of stuff has been retained from Civilization: Through The Ages, some thrown out and new elements introduced. It's much easier to bring to the table for beginners because the pace of the game has been improved. For lovers of Civilization: Through The Ages players it's probably not going to take the place of that game, but there might be room for both in the collection due to Nations being a bit more accessible to a wider audience. (Indeed the box is so big my Civilization: Through The Ages box now resides inside my Nations box.) Pity the box is double the size it needs to be. The rules are well done and the game is easy to pick up. I'll forgive them the thin boards if it means (like Castles of Burgundy), that we see some expansion nation boards sometime in the future. It's the sort of Civ game I was bound to like and I'm glad they've openly borrowed and refined a fine game into something more accessible.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
