RATING: 9
Before Nations, my only real experience with a civ game happened 3 months prior with Sid Meier's Civilization: The Board Game and I didn't especially like it. But I like Nations. A lot.

When I first read the rules to Nations, I thought that this might be a solid 7 or 8. Then after our first 2-player game, it felt like a high 7, but probably not an 8. Then we played a second time, and I thought this might be an 8, actually. And then after our third play yesterday, I'm thinking it might be a 9.

I'm not sure if Nations is actually a civ game. There's not map to expand. The military is very indirect. There's quite a bit of tactical decision to make based on the cards that appear, which don't always fit logically together. But this is all why I probably like it a lot. The worker placement is really well-done. And the economics/resource management is exactly what I like.

Because there's such a slow build at the beginning, and by the end you feel like your nation is just starting to become powerful and productive, you don't want the game to end. I can tell there's still a lot of plays left in this one for me.

RATING: 6.5
This is an interesting civ game, although I miss the greater feeling of technological paths found in other titles like Through the Ages. It feels more like I'm tearing down all of my old infrastructure and building new, better infrastructure than like I'm upgrading in Nations. 

The game largely revolves around jockeying for position for the events, for book scoring, for military/stability for wars and player order, and for progress cards. It's a very tactical game, and you'll need to adjust on the fly to what comes up.

1 play, 3er, 3 ages

I love civ games! It started with Sid Meier's Civ II way back in the '90s, but I've since tried many board game versions. [thing=71][/thing], the few times I've played it, has been a blast -- but it sure is long! I didn't enjoy [thing=77130][/thing] very much: seemed as if your game play was dictated too much by your starting civ, and I didn't like the totally random board layout? [thing=40765][/thing] was better, but I didn't like the random objective cards and totally random events. My favorite has been [thing=25613][/thing], nearly perfect except for having to spend so much effort keeping up militarily (and the fact that I am such a bad player, despite having played nearly 200 games!). 

The game rules are quite straightforward. It feels much like Through the Ages, but plays faster and with less emphasis on military strength. It is a lighter game, though: you won't see all the cards and there's a less predictable progression so you can't make definite plans; you must react to the game situation turn by turn, much more so than in TTA. At this point I still prefer TTA, but would be happy to play this again -- especially if time is limited! It seems to be an excellent lighter civ game!

RATING: N/A
1-5, 120m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game feels like the next step for gamers from 7 Wonders.  It is a civilization building game of sorts but everything is represented with cards.  Each turn players take cards of different types in turn order from a grid, these cards depict historical people, constructions (called Wonders), military units, buildings, and wars throughout history.  These cards are added to your tableau where they produce resources each turn.  Just describing it this way you should see the similarities to 7 Wonders.  This game takes it a step further though for those of us that want a deeper game than that very friendly gateway.

Past the 7 Wonders-esque tableau building this game becomes mean.  Players need to actually deploy workers to their cards to use many of them.  Deploying workers and building Wonders costs resources.  These resources need to be managed because every turn events happen that cost these resources and many hurt the player(s) with the least.  Many of the cards are also lost, or at the very least their effects are, if you fall behind in a certain resource.  Players can start wars where more is lost if you don't at least match their military prowess.

You certainly need a high resolve to play this game because there is a decent amount of player interaction and your opponents will come after you and it is going to hurt.  On top of which, the game is a decent length.  The rulebook says 40 minutes per player and I think that hits the nail right on the head.  I should mention though that I think the player count should stay at 3-4.  Playing with 5 the rules adjust and negative events hit more people and it makes it even more hopeless, especially for newer players.  Head-to-head and positive/negative events are usually going to split so early mistakes could become exacerbated without the ability to overcome them.

I gotta say, I really like this game.  It is certainly less fiddly than other civ builder games and plays a lot faster (not to mention sets up and breaks down a hell of a lot faster).  Plus with everything being diluted to a card-driven system while strategies are hard to come by without a few plays under your belt it is mechanically simpler than other civ builders as well.  My issue in the past with civ builders is that they feel very similar to computer civ builders and I'd much rather play a computer game without all the fiddliness and set-up/clean-up.  This game has that flavor but gives a little bit different of a feel that makes me want to play it and I don't have to spend so much time on overhead.  Also, the game has a decent amount of variety with hundreds of cards which should keep it fresh across multiple plays.  I also like the integrated difficulty mechanic that allows stronger players to play with a handicap against weaker players.

I almost hate that I like it so much because the learning curve is Everest steep and it is a mean and decently long game.  It is very hard to get people to finish (let alone play again) a game that beats them up and takes a while to do it.  Which is a weird catch-22 because the only way to figure out this game is to give it a couple plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I can see myself playing Nations much more often than TTA and I would consider it a good addition to my game collection, mainly because it solves the 'fast paced civ game' problem we, gamers, are living from a long time now.

RATING: 4
TTA inspired card game? Yes purlease...

And after half a game it does feel like a TTA tribute band. In a good way. Less fiddly, wars handled well. Similar decision making to TTA, less Computer Civ. inspired but smooth game play with little downtime. Want to explore a lot more. For experienced gamers or TTA lovers go straight to the advanced deck.

3 games in.

Main difference to TTA is that not all cards will come out. Which makes it a more tactical game and in some ways more enjoyable because you don't know exactly how history will unfold. It does NOT though seem to create a strategic arc - the points at the end are incremental not exponential as per TTA. This can be both a good and a bad thing. Not keen on artwork - however everything is functional.

The effects of war can be motivated by stability (apart from vp) or by a weak country taking a war. This is both elegant and so show unsatisfactory compared to TTA where the threat of war was a powerful effect on players decisions, here it's more of a euro nerf. Going 1st is worth having though as is the event bonus.

In last age there was a sense of just buying buildings and shuffling around workers for more VP - this might be inexperience with Nations but it felt a bit gamey.

Overall a good 1st design effort but if you are going to step in to the ring with the heavy weight champion of Civ then a few knocks have to expected.

5th  play

I'd rather play TTA - this is too long for a lighter game and the ages arc is not very interesting (actually its not really there_- just 8 similar rounds. There might be a lot of cards but they are not very different from each other so the thrill of the draft is lost. I would love to see Vlaada tackle Civ again.

6th play

Not unpleasant to play. However v tactical and no arc. Nothing wrong per se but but being so close to TTA give me TTA

7th

Grim.

---------------------------------

And Vivienne Raper has written a perfect description of Nations vis a vis TTA

'How much do you enjoy civilisation games versus resource management euros? 

..... Nations feels (to me) more like Russian Railroads than Civilisation on the PC. TTA is Civilisation for board gamers without the map.

TTA, because every card comes out during the game, can afford to have very distinctive, rich and unusual cards. More like the card richness in Innovation, but with a lot more strategy, less take-that, etc. than in that game. Because different cards come up in every game of Nations, most cards have to simply produce something. Otherwise, you'd need lots of take-that (as with Innovation) to stop a runaway leader if someone drew a particularly good card.

For this reason, Nations felt - to me - much more like building an economic engine than building a civilisation. TTA has the same economic engine buzzing away underneath, but it lots more other cool civ stuff going on as well. To me, that felt like 'building a civilisation' and not 'generating victory points'. Depending how much you like raw economic engine games, your mileage with Nations will differ.'

-------------------------

and am done with Nations.

RATING: 6
Initial rating 7.

I had hopes for this one but unfortunately didn't meet my expectations. It is an interesting civ game but i found it flawed in several aspects that made me not wanting to play it anymore.

- A common tactic i disliked was the "tactical pass": Just do a cheap action and wait until others pass so you can reassign workers (military, stability) to harm others and/or gain the event bonuses

- The uncertainty that a type of card will appear during an age is something i found irritating and random.
It wasn't too uncommon that only 1 military card appeared during a new age which led to a position others couldn't respond. 
The problem was worse if your strategy involves some future cards that never appear. For example a civilization or leader that benefits from golden age cards and only very few appear.

12/3/2015: Dropped rating to 6. I don't feel like playing this any time soon.

RATING: 7.75
Very fun and streamlined Civ building game. Very enjoyable although I have a few major issues with it. There is a large over emphasis on military as it gets you nearly every other resource in the game. Battles essentially let you convert gold into other resources, colonies give you free goods, wars make enemies spend valuable resources (either to catch up in military, to take the war before you, or to build up large amounts of Stability), the numerous military related events, and the fact military determines turn order.

It felt like you *must* have a strong military to do well in this game. If the event cards don't heavily favor military and have many "lowest stability goes last" it seems to help balance it out, but it still seems overly strong.

Not sure on a "fix" to it, but maybe have military + stability determine turn order? Still fun and I want to play it again a few times.

Plus, I wish the game went into the modern era or even early future, but was kind of long enough as it was. This is kind of just a minor nitpick though.

UPDATE: The problems I feared are a lot worse than I first thought. Military is so dominantly strong and lopsided that it makes the game less enjoyable. Additionally, you really need to come up with some system for balancing the cards if you're using Advanced and Expert. Something like base cards + X amount of others and also discarding some cards if you don't meet a certain number of each type. Hope the designers can come up with a fix and official rules for this!

RATING: 9
Commendable civilisation card/worker-placement game. Players are required to purchase cards that bestow certain benefits on their owners, such as increasing their production capacity for certain resources, progressing them along the game's various tracks e.g. military strength or national stability, the ability to start a war, construct a wonder, acquire an advisor etc. Most of the cards need to be activated in order to be useful and this is where the worker placement element comes into play, requiring a balance of knowing when to deploy extra workers thereby increasing production but also introducing added demands to the nation. 'Nations' is an extremely accomplished game, reminiscent of 'Saint Petersburg' in its card-drafting mechanism, but with a lot more depth to what you can do with your cards once you've acquired them. There's a hint of the rich get richer here and players can easily get left behind, especially if they get on the wrong end of a few wars, but the multiple paths to victory make this a highly recommended civilisation game.

RATING: 6
It does have the buzz, but it is not a grand epic civ game. It is a Euro with a pasted on civ-theme.

It is a decent Euro, placing your workers to gather better placement options, with some timed requirements (the event cards) mixed in. Player interaction is very limited (only wars and maybe some as of yet unseen other event cards) and therefore it plays like any other well designed Euro. Which I have enough of already. 

Would not mind owning it, but I'm not looking for it either.

[edit] Played Through The Ages (TTA) yesterday again, and Nations is clearly almost a 1-1 copy of that game. So why do I like TTA very much while I'm left quite cold at this one? Been thinking about it and the big clincher is that in TTA, you can upgrade your workers to better versions, while in Nations, you simpy remove their old job and get something else instead. There is no 'upgrading', just 'tactical placement'. And by removing this mechanism, the game went from strategic to tactic. Which is fine for a Euro-game, but not for a civ game. That should the epitome of strategic play.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is obviously heavily inspired by Through the Ages.  Nations modernizes its older brother to some degree by streamlining some processes, shortening the play time, and the use of tokens instead of sliding markers up and down tracks is an improvement.  They're pretty much even in terms of strategic and tactical decisions.

But I prefer Through the Ages, despite the fact that there are more hurdles to playing it.  I think it does a better job of capturing the civilization theme, it's more interactive and geared towards aggression, there's more opportunity for ebb and flow because players get so many more actions over the course of the game, and, frankly, Nations is quite derivative and I tend to give more respect to a wholly inventive design.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Exceptionally well as a resource management game, not particularly exceptional in terms of being a civ-building game (totally neglecting technologies in terms of unique game-changing mechanics and in parts rather odd resource combinations of the buildings make for that). The civ-building part is [-]rather abstract[/-]. When coming to think of it, this game is completely devoid of civ-building. Nevertheless the game feels fresh and comes with a ton of great ideas, some subtle,some others less subtle but very direct. The wars for example are a great way of directly influencing your opponents next move(s),with the opponents still having lots of options at hand(thus eliminating a scripted game walkthrough). Increasing your military strength will leave your opponents with the choice to either:
- Buy wars and thus be spared from negative effects at the cost of other options or
- try to increase your military strength if available
- Try to increase your stability to neglect the effects of wars to a certain degree
- neglect the threats of war and hope the damage done won't be as decisive as the benefit you gained from the alternative.

There's so much more in this box (like the integrated handicap variant - brilliant touch), but after just one game I can't quite put my finger on it. I just know that I was amazed during several moments over the course of the game by just how game elements are connected. This is a great game that deserves coming back to.In terms of civilization-building it's nowhere close to recent Sid Meier' Civ, but it's rather in the veins of Through the ages. Over which it clearly wins. Administering your resources is by far less time-consuming than in TtA and thus less dry. And here's my 2 cents on the artwork. Before I had access to the game and looking at the pictures here I thought the artwork sucked. However, in the context of a game in play the artwork is perfectly fine, even some really great winter/autumn sky backgrounds to give you some mood on some cards. With this many distinct cards you can't expect every card to show tons of detail. And in my opinion making a distinct illustration for each and every one of these many cards is going the extra mile.Actually every time I play I come t appreciate the artwork even more.   

In the end it boils down to a very "simple" resource management and combi building/combi management game (every building mixes two different resource types, start-buildings produce a combination of 2 different resources and for each age the buildings produce exactly one more resource, i.e. 3,4,5 or - finally -6 resources in age IV) Despite these very obvious optimization traits, I still like this game. Furthermore I can ignore that this game doesn't deliver on the civ-building aspect. 

However if you're craving for that real civ-building experience, a game where civ-building expresses in more ways than just the name of the cards with some background info, I strongly advise you to go with Sid Meier's Civ or Clash of Cultures. Make this the game of your choice when looking for a great Euro!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Completely replaced Through the Ages. I enjoyed the gameplay of TtA, but the amount of rules and play time were way too much. Nations is only a little shorter, but the rules are vastly superior. All of the complicated, hard-to-remember rules of TtA have now been streamlined in Nations. I watched Rahdo's runthrough, skimmed through the rulebook, and had no issues playing Nations. TtA, on the other hand, I tried to play multiple times and it was always an all-day affair just flipping through the rulebook. We aborted most of our games of TtA because it just took way too long.

The resources in Nations are represented by tokens, so no more having to figure out if the blue cubes represent food or other resources. No more being destroyed by an unexpected military card - nations handles military in a way that is much easier to grasp and more fair to all players. You can even start a war as a defensive move in Nations if you're trailing in military (assuming that your opponent doesn't start a war first, of course).

The only downsides are the insane price and some component issues. There is no reason at all that this game should cost $68, but that is the current price. The components are fairly cheap (thin player boards, cards that feel cheap) and there aren't any more pieces than other games that cost much less, and the publisher isn't some small unknown company. This game should definitely be in the $30-$40 range. I have no idea how they're trying to justify that price.
Also, the cards are all badly cut. There are so many cards that you wouldn't be able to remember which is which based on how they're cut, so this won't affect gameplay, but for $68 I would expect much better quality. The cards also look and feel kind of cheap. I would sleeve them, but considering they don't get held in a player's hand they should hold up okay.

Overall, a great game. I imagine that they'll eventually release a version that fixes the production issues and is a more reasonable price, at which point this game should definitely replace Through the Ages in the rankings.

::UPDATE:: 2/5/13
I played a second game only a couple weeks after the first. I'm only now getting to updating my thoughts. It's a fun game, but it's nowhere near as good as TtA. In fact, if they didn't come out and blatantly say it, I wouldn't even think that they were shooting for a "quicker TtA". Instead, I would just think they borrowed a couple mechanics from the king of civ-builders (who can blame them). 

The game ends up more of a turn-by-turn tactical struggle, rather than a grand-strategy, building game. And this is where it loses quite few points for me. With a civ-builder, I want to have a heavy dose of multi-turn planning and strategizing. I don't really get it here. Instead, I take a look at the event for this turn, then look at the cards and my current status, and do everything I can to best position myself this turn, without worrying about future rounds. The player that consistently does this best throughout the game will be the winner.

In that regard, I find it to be a completely different game than TtA. The game I prefer to compare it to is [b]Peloponnes[/b]. Peloponnes, like Nations, has events that can do serious damage if you don't prepare for them. It also has that turn-by-turn tactical feel because player get a new tile each round, determined by an auction phase. There are other similarities, but I'll spare them for this arena. Suffice it to say that this game is NOT Through the Ages [i]light[/i]. Nor is it TtA [i]elegant[/i], [i]streamlined[/i], or [i]II[/i]. It's not TtA [i]anything[/i].

RATING: 9
Best civilization game I've played so far.

An elegant, highly rewarding experience. No downtime, tough choices, high interaction, random set-up each game. Either equal or variable start, in any case no two games will be the same. The drafting mechanism combined with the solitaire worker placement is extremelly simple, yet extremelly tactical mechanism. The game creates constantly the sense of urgency in several fields at the same time (you need culture which is a competitive track to score vps, you need military/stability to keep up with wars, you need money to get the edge on the draft, you need resources to place your workers and build wonders, you need architects to build wonders, you need food and stability to keep your population content and you have an event each round which places even more "stress" to the round).

I think this is by far the best civilization game, and it dwarves even the previous behemoth, Through the Ages (which is also a fine game, but suffers from extreme downtime)

RATING: 7
What I like: 

-The unique roles, the feeling of growing your empire through the ages, abstracted military as opposed to in your face miniatures. Very nice.
-Lots of complex strategic decisions.
-Lots of replay value from the factions to the massive amount of cards that vary from game to game. 
-The game is actual much more tactical than strategic. You don't get to say "I'm doing this for the entire game", you have to switch up your strategy based on what cards are drawn. And it's not actually that difficult to constantly change your plans in this. 


What I dislike:

-It's mean. Too mean. You can rise together or fall together in this game. If you are willing to sacrifice what it takes to win the rewards for the round, then you can really drag everyone down with you and make them suffer. It's not like it leaves you in a much better position than them,but it's always an option if you feel it benefits you more than them this time. But if you are not very careful you can get screwed over completely by a small mistake and an opponent who smells blood. There are just some folks I can't play this with cause it's so mean. 
-It's a bit too long.

[imageid=554375]

This makes me think it wasn't meant to be played 2 players [i]really[/i]. It's like the game was designed for 3 to 5 and they threw a solo variant but on the box just printed 1-5. It's a pretty lackluster game with only 2 players. It can suffer because of how few cards hit the board. If a ton of war cards come out and only 1 colony or 1 leader well someone is not going to get a leader or colony that turn. Surprisingly I find it's fine at 3 but best at 4 or 5.

RATING: 7
My rating on this one is a little tentative as I think I really need another play to do the game justice. This game feels a lot like Through the Ages, a game I like a lot despite the fact that it's almost exactly the perfect recipe for me to hate. Nations is a lot less fiddly than TTA, but as it turns out, the fiddliness of TTA isn't really something that bothers me that much.

My biggest concern in TTA is also my biggest concern with Nations, which is that it seems like you have to play a military strategy or risk getting stuck in a bad position. Now maybe that's not true with Nations, but it really felt like it. I know that I didn't play this game particularly well, so maybe that's all it was, but I know in my next play I'm going to do my best to do military well.

I think that as long as I know that the game is unforgiving and I'm aware of the fact that you have to play the military game, I'll enjoy this much like I do TTA.

The one thing I found really wonky in this game was the five player rule that converts "best" into "first and second best" and "worst" into "first and second worst". That and the fact that ties count for worst and not for best ended up causing strange situations where players were tied for both best and worst in some categories. It also seemed to disincentivize the worst player from ever trying to improve their standing as you had to not only pass the next player, but also the player above that, as a tie for second worst is still worst. 

Edit:My second play really redeemed this game for me!

Five players was too many for this game, especially with the wonky five player rule which just adds strangeness. Three players made for a very tight competitive game. My concerns about military were largely erased as well. I think the rule we missed about negative stability mitigates the chances of anyone playing a military-only strategy. And it's entirely possible, and probably a good idea, to disband your military for a few turns and still come back to field a new and improved army that's competitive.

I'm really looking forward to more plays, something I couldn't really say after the first botched game. I'm definitely a lot more excited to get this to the table than TTA. I like TTA, but I'd rather play it online than over the board, whereas this is a lot of fun in person.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
[thing=126042][/thing] is a civilization game with smooth sailing for the civilizations: dealing with resources is easier than in TTA and Sid Meier's; the military part, although still with relevance for colonization, events and (indirect) wars, is quite friendly, so much so that many events don't even benefit the highest military, as well as that the stability of a nation can reduce the impact of wars to almost nothing, except the loss of 1vp.

With a weakened military, which has more impact in turn order and conquering colonies, and without a trading or commerce mechanic (always a bummer not have one in civ games), [thing=126042][/thing] ends up being more a solitaire exercize of tableau development. Therefore, is possible to have a runaway leader, with few chances of being stopped by actions of the others - still, this is better than bashing the leader, or crippling the game of someone by constant attacks.

The cards seem balanced, in cost, space for workers and use. However, since almost 2/3 of the cards of an Era stay out of the game, which adds variety and replay value, it also makes [thing=126042][/thing] more unpredictable, tatical and, possibly, uneven, as some type of cards may show too rarely.

[thing=126042][/thing] is functional, but I expect more from a good civ game. Even more than in TTA, [thing=126042][/thing] felt more abstract - the correspondence between what a card it, what it cost and what it does is, many times, thin at best, and, in others, downright strange: for instance, it costs more stones and the same number of workers to build the Sphinx than to build the Great Wall of China.

I like the variety provided by the different nations - the balance between than is another matter, one I'm not equiped to talk about. They are good for a replay value aspect.

[thing=126042][/thing], if played in a good pace, is more dynamic than its competitors, and it fits in more limited frames of available time.

In the end, [thing=126042][/thing] pleased less than I thought. It is more solitaire and abstract than I like, and the options seem more limited. I'm not  a person that enjoys the predominance the military has on TTA, still, I prefer it due to a more organic development of the civilizations. Going further, when compared with other civ games, [thing=126042][/thing] falls behind the class - sure, it lasts less, but it also give less. [thing=126042][/thing] isn't a bad game, just way more ordinary than I wanted it to be.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A bit simpler than Through the Ages and with more pleasing player interaction. Instead of all the tiny discs from TtA you just have a maximum of 13 chunky worker meeples to place on your board.

I vastly prefer the simple war/stability stuff in this game as opposed to what TtA did. I was never even sure how TtA was intended to play here.

Also, the card market, at first glance seemingly a direct ripoff of TtA, replenishes more slowly, so that it actually feels like a source of player interaction. Comparisons to 7 Wonders must be looking at this piece. You may have to be quick to grab what you need and you can even hate draft.

But for some reason I'm not getting the "just one more turn" feel of TtA. I have no idea why not. I'll probably have to play TtA again to get an idea. Is it too hard to have an informed guess about what may happen in the future, in Nations? Or is something about the game state too simple?

I do think that Nations is more likely to get played than TtA just because it's much easier to learn and relearn its rules.

...Maybe it's that the economic system is too simple? It's straightforward and I don't wonder afterwards about the possibilities. Nations also often feels like an iterated majorities game over military, stability, and books. There is not really any change of feel over the course of the game, which deprives it of any epic feeling. To me the defining characteristic of a civilization game is the qualitative change in how the game plays over its course. (But I don't rate TtA too highly here either.)

...Yes, after revisiting TtA I think the interest of that game comes from the logistical puzzle of deciding what you should do within one turn, between interruptions in the form of maintenance (corruption especially) and unpredictability in the form of the card market and player interaction. I think a higher percentage of Nations' play is in the card drafting race and the majorities, with the MPS economic management component of the game being much simpler.

So why does TtA have a "just one more turn" feel to it: Because the stuff you decided to delay, and to not do this turn, is still on your mind, and there's an urge to keep playing and "mop up" those loose ends. But in so doing you keep making more loose ends. I think Nations has less of these "loose ends." Usually the end of a round feels like a reasonable stopping point.

RATING: 9
Civ builder game that is a deeper cousin to 7 Wonders meets Puerto Rico with a dash of London.  The level of planning required is at a fairly high level, so be prepared to think.  Despite the apparent complexity of the board and card layout, the game mechanics are straight forward and easy to master.  Where you will need to stretch your brain is planning your build, what to develop and the cost vs benefit ratio of paying for one thing over another or to save resources for future rounds.

On the A side vs B side dilemma: Asymmetric is cool from the concept of alternative paths to victory but more challenging to balance.  Even with 7 Wonders the mix of Wonders in play typically favors some strategies over others merely due to the competition for certain cards.  The A sides will force players to find their own way and should lead to alternate paths due to the weight and options of Nations

From a fluff perspective I like that you can create your own Civ.  For example, in one game I had Pyramids, Hanging Gardens, Aqueducts, Cathedrals, and Easter Island statues all accessible from the Suez Canal like an archeological Disneyland or the Las Vegas Strip.  I also had Abraham Lincoln as an advisor but he was killed on the last turn.  Sometimes the cost of freedom is high...

There's not much innovation here. If you've played Through the Ages, I could teach you to play this game in less than 10 minutes.

It's very similar to Through the Ages. Several mechanics felt copied straight from it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
-Opinion:
A top-notch civ game for the hobby-gamer. Complexity is well above introduction eurogames but far beneath heavy games. Game seems to be very balanced between military/stability tactic in that both will get benefits. Also every choice seems meaningful because that card you wanted may not be available the next turn. This also causes the game to be more a tactical game and less about optimizing strategies around certain cards which is how I prefer it, I am not the super-optimizing gamer type. The game itself plays out as a game of balancing an unstable resource engine and often you feel the same "feeding anxiety" as in Agricola in a good way. The ages also creep power so it makes switching tactic mid-game feasible when you anyway have to refresh your engine to keep up with the rest. The things I would have wished more from was the art and perhaps even more asymmetry in the advanced production cards. Rules are easy to explain and playtime with 4p, setup and rules-explanation is about 2,5 hours.

-Other thoughts:
Tweaked the decks after my second game to introduce more asymmetry by systematically switching basic cards for interesting int. and adv. cards and trying to keep overall resource balance. Worked well in making the game more interesting but as I also added one more of each category card into each age to allow for 5p game I diluted the decks a bit. This again caused some card-streaks that in one case screwed the ones relying on colonies in that in the first three ages only 5/15 colonies came out. How the decks are chosen will greatly influence the game experience so tune them to your liking, all from predictable and symmetrically balanced to chaotic streaky decks are possible depending on the deck buildup.

-Comments on others comments and reviews:
I assume that many people bashing the game have not even played it, have made serious rule errors or are TtA fanboys who must rip on all other civ-game copies because naturally TtA is da best in everything and there can not and should not be made any attempts at improvements or streamlining. This assumption is because most of the negative things I heard spoken of were nowhere to be found or seem to be extremely easily mitigated if one even has the slightest idea about how the mechanics function. The one negative thing I agree on is that bookkeeping is not always easy, but that can easily be mitigated by treating everything systematically, e.g. producing in the exact order shown on player aids. I also recognize that the game is not for everyone, if you want to be able to predict a game and optimize your strategy according to that then this is not for you, this is tactical and the strategy will have to be in constant motion.

-Comparison to TtA: TtA is way more evil and convoluted. Nations has less possibilities but feels more streamlined and better as a game. Also the military suckiness of TtA is fixed in Nations.

-Arcane Tinmen Small sleeves

RATING: 8

RATING: 7
I was inclined to pooh-pooh Nations after reading the rules, because it draws from [gameid=25613] so much that it almost feels like there should be an additional design credit to Vlaada Chvátil.  When I actually played Nations, however, it became clear that the card design is different enough to make it feel like a different beast.  The framework is similar, but there's a much different strategic and tactical feel, and none of the cards feel derivative.  In addition, it's easier to teach, and the military strategy is addressed in a much better fashion here.  Nations also has that wonderful mechanic where every victory point matters.  Getting or losing one VP is not some meaningless exercise, but is a big deal that may mean the difference between victory and a loss.  

That said, this game has balance problems, including a severe runaway leader issue.  Military is so strong that it's incredibly punitive, and once you're on the bottom, you're probably bleeding points to the winner.  What's more, with turn order being military, you're frequently going to be unable to grab a technology to turn it around, meaning that you just play out the string if you fall behind.

As far as Civ goes, there are lots of worse ones out there.  There's a looseness to this game which is quite appealing, and the sheer number of cards makes sessions vary much more than in a lot of Civ games.  I wouldn't buy this counting on being a forever classic, but you'll get an easy few games out of it that are quite entertaining.

RATING: N/A
sembra + un gestionale che un gioco di civilizzazione, preferisco TTA. 

While Through the Ages already took out the dudes on a map leaving conflict-loving gamers wondering if this still is a civ game, Nations goes a step further and is the "euro-ized" version of Through the Ages. No direct conflict, streamlined mechanisms, short player turns and playing time at the cost of strategic depth and variety. Hardcore BGG gamers will continue to favour Through the Ages, my vote as a euro gamer averse to direct player targetting in euro games goes for Nations.

RATING: 4
I don't know how to rate this game. It's good at being what it is, but I don't want that thing.

I mainly game two-player with my husband and we're equally matched skill-wise. We play fast, don't have AP, enjoy replaying games and are not adverse to direct conflict.

For this reason, I love Through the Ages. It's brutal, strategic, conflict-heavy, unique among modern heavy board games, and rewards repeat plays. Many of the mechanics raise intriguing possibilities, such as the event-stacking. All the wonders and leaders are very very different.

I bought Nations at about the same time and, coming back to it after several plays of TTA, it's not in the same ballpark. It's JASE in TTA clothing - a VP engine worker-placement game with numerous very similar cards and limited direct conflict. It's overly forgiving, bland and a bit 'meh'. I can't see any occasion when I'd rather play Nations than TTA. As such, it's for trade.

That said, if I were in a large cult-of-the-new group with numerous newbies and many players with AP, Nations would be a far better choice. It has large clear boards, which are far too large for two, but probably brilliant with five. It's easy to teach because the mechanics are immediately familiar to anyone who has played Caylus and it's many imitators. You can play it once and not lose miserably, and gives a cut-down TTA-type civ feel... etc. etc. If this sounds like your gaming group, I'd rate this game a 9 or 10. 

For us, though, it's a 4. When I'm in a large group with newbies, I far prefer negotiation/trading/bluffing games like I'm the Boss or Tammany Hall to this type of heads-down soulless resource-conversion fest. Large groups of strangers are simply friends you haven't met yet, after all! :)

I'M CURRENTLY TRYING TO TRADE THIS HOT GAME FOR AN OLD GERMAN-STYLE OOP GAME. I'M ESPECIALLY INTERESTED IN WINNER'S CIRCLE and CASTLE. Happy to pay any difference in price. My copy of Nations is in like-new condition, played three times.

RATING: 7
Nations is the perfect Euro take on the popular Civilization category of games. A lot of people call it Through the Ages light and I think that is an apt description. It's still a fairly heavy game, but it focuses more on building a tableau of point scoring workers than the usual tech tree/conquest engines found in most other Civ games.

The only thing that bugs me is the rather small amount of civilizations included in the box. Asymmetrical starting points and powers are one of my favorite features in these sort of games and while Nations has them, there just isn't enough of them. Hopefully an expansion will come out soon to up the count, because other than that little limitation, this is a damn fine game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[i]Update: Sold it only because it competed directly with Through The Ages in my collection, I just don't need two civ-building games.[/i]

Looks dreadful, but otherwise this is a good game with a ton of tough decisions to be made. I really like the huge variety of cards. Also, I like how designers minimized downtime here, Nations is more dynamic than Through The Ages, the flow of the round is as smooth as it gets. 

But I have to say that it feels more like a worker placement/resource management game rather than a "civilization builder". Unlike Through The Ages where you have different priorities at various stages of the game, here there's no real sense of progress, each era feels pretty much the same as you focus on the exact same things all the time. The numbers get higher, but the game "doesn't tell a story". 

Keep in mind that this game is very unforgiving - Nations is extremely prone to the "snowball effect". If you don't get your resource engine running properly fast enough, it will be almost impossible for you to catch up with the others. Sometimes the winner can be clearly determined halfway through the game (especially in 2P games).

While not perfect, Nations still offers a satisfying gaming experience. The solo variant works like a charm!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6
While we were in Essen, we saw a lot of people carrying the huge box of Nations. We went to the publishers booth and noticed that the tables it was played on, were filled with people all the time. The pricetag of EUR60,- made it also a game we wouldn’t buy before we tried it.

But then it happened. Only a couple of weeks after Spiel, the price dropped with almost 50% in Germany. There was only a limited amount available, watched a review on BGG, and we didn’t hesitate and ordered a copy. The only thing that worried us a bit, was the delivery-time. 3-7 weeks. In the worst case, it would make it to our home just before Christmas. That would make it a great Christmas gift for M. to give to P. After a little more then 3 weeks the copy of Nations already arrived. P. didn’t want to wait. M. just said wait until Christmas. As always a man has to listen to his wife, so I did, and before I knew there it was. Christmas. Time to play nations.

I knew this wouldn’t be an easy family game. When I opened the box, the rulebook smiled to me. 24 pages. Oops, that’s a little more paper then I expected. Started to read the rules, and I immediately discovered the first good thing about this game. Well written rules. Everybody knows well written rules are a big problem the last couple of years. In fact it’s such a big problem that you will be surprised if you find a game with well written rules inside. The rules are easy to understand, and very clear. You hardly will have to read back because you don’t understand what they’re talking about.

The other very nice thing of this game, are the custom shape tokens. What an improvement compared to other games (like Alea games, which have the same shape of components for almost everything (VP’s are small hexagons (Louis XIV, Notre Dame, Augsburg 1520))). Minor aspect of this, is the fact that it will take more time to punch them out of the cardboard, and you will have to be more careful, otherwise the pieces might get damaged.

Then the cards, I was not happy with them. They are standard cards, nothing wrong about that, but in the right bottom corners (and if I say corner, I mean corner), there are some tiny (and if I say tiny, I mean very tiny) symbols on some of the cards (the advanced and the expert cards). These symbols have about the size of 1/20 of an inch, which are placed about the same distance from the corner. Normally I wouldn’t discover when a card is misaligned a little bit (1/30″). But in this case, it makes a difference, because the tiny symbols did partly fall of the edge, and this way it was even harder to recognize them. Also you’ll have to sort all of the cards (by type and age) before your first game. It took us about 15 minutes. Not a big deal, but why don’t place them in the right order after printing them?

As said, rules are very clear. The game takes 4 periods to play and each period will take 2 turns. So there are 8 rounds to play, and each round will take 3 phases. Phase one is the Maintenance Phase, and will take about 2 minutes. Phase two is the Action Phase, and this is the phase were it’s all about. Phase three is the Resolution Phase, and will also take only a couple of minutes.

Phase two, the Action Phase, is the phase where you will spend the most time. During this phase you can do one of the three available actions:

    Buy a progress card. You can buy a progress card from one of the three rows on the progress board. 1st row will cost 1 gold. 2nd row, 2 gold and 3rd row – 3 gold. When you buy a progress card you place it on your player-board (buildings, military, colonies, advisor and wonders), place it on the scoring board (war) or resolve it immediately (battle, golden age. The number of places on your player-board is limited per card type, but you can always destroy a previous placed card.
    Place a worker. You can place one of your workers on one of your buildings/military cards on your player board. Some cards will produce resources at the end of the round, while other cards will give you stability or military-power immediately. Placing a working will cost you stone. Some of the cards have cost to upkeep it at the end of the round.
    Hire an architect. Architects are needed to build the wonders. Some wonders will need 1 architect, others will need more. Architects are taken from the common pool on the scoring board.

During the Action Phase, every player takes on of the possible actions. Then the next player is on turn. This takes on, until every player has passed.

When a period ends, there is a scoring round. A player scores 1VP for every other player that is behind him on the heritage track on the scoring board.

Basically this is all there is to this game. Not very difficult, and once you understand the iconography, it’s pretty clear.

This is not a typical P&M game. It’s no secret that we like medium-weight family-games the most. But we were very curious to play a civilization game. And I think that it wasn’t a bad choice to become Nations the first experience in the genre. It isn’t a very hard game to play. It isn’t a brain burner. It plays pretty fast (I’m sure you’ll manage to play in 40-45 minutes per player after a couple of plays). You’ll get the feeling that you are developing your Nation. The mechanics feels logical and makes sense. After all, I only can say, this is a P&M game. We like it very much, and could recommend it to everybody who wants to play a heavier game then a family game is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is a difficult one for me, as there are parts of this game I quite enjoy.

Through the Ages 'lite' it is not. It dumps a lot of TtA stuff, but. Can still fall in the 3 to 4 hour range with 4+ players.

My biggest gripes are that military seems grossly overpowered, allowing you to go first each turn, be free of the fear of war, gain good beneifts from battles and colonies, AND benefit from the occasional random event. This leads to rich get richer situation as they always get the pick of the crop. And unfortunately, the poor tend to get poorer too. This game lacks any kind of 'catch up' mechanic, and an early mistake, or even poor first turn can mean you have zero chance of winning but you go through the motions anyway. Hell, I'd say generally speaking you could call the eventual winner of the game after the first scoring turn - and there's literally nothing you can do about stopping a runaway leader.

Random card draws and ridiculously crippling random events round off the triumverate of bad news.

And a slight negative on top of all this, it doesn't really *feel* like you're building a civilisation, more an engine to get the highest score. That's fine, but don't come expecting to find a game rich in theme (pasted on is probably more appropriate).

I find the war mechanism interesting though, and there are some elegant mechanics in play, even if they aren't perhaps best realised in this.

Sadly, I can't score this game higher than 5 - and tbh, that feels a bit generous.
